Extracting Answer from: 
Since the slope of the line is $\frac{2}{5},$ the line rises 2 units vertically for every 5 horizontal units.  Thus, a possible direction vector is $\begin{pmatrix} 5 \\ 2 \end{pmatrix}.$

[asy]
unitsize(1 cm);

pair A, B, C;

A = (0,0);
B = (5,0);
C = (5,2);

draw(A--B--C);
draw(A--C,red,Arrow(6));

label("$5$", (A + B)/2, S);
label("$2$", (B + C)/2, E);
[/asy]

This means any nonzero scalar multiple of $\begin{pmatrix} 5 \\ 2 \end{pmatrix}$ is a possible direction vector.  The possible options are then $\boxed{\text{B, E, G}}.$

Since the slope of the line is $\frac{2}{5},$ any vector in the direction of the line must be of the form $\begin{pmatrix} 5k \\ 2k \end{pmatrix}$ for some scalar $k.$  Checking each of the options, we see that:

(A) $\begin{pmatrix} 2 \\ 5 \end{pmatrix}$ is not in the form $\begin{pmatrix} 5k \\ 2k \end{pmatrix}.$
(B) $\begin{pmatrix} 5 \\ 2 \end{pmatrix}$ is in the form $\begin{pmatrix} 5k \\ 2k \end{pmatrix}$ (with $k = 1$).
(C) $\begin{pmatrix} 0 \\ 0 \end{pmatrix}$ is not in the form $\begin{pmatrix} 5k \\ 2k \end{pmatrix}.$
(D) $\begin{pmatrix} -2 \\ -5 \end{pmatrix}$ is not in the form $\begin{pmatrix} 5k \\ 2k \end{pmatrix}.$
(E) $\begin{pmatrix} -5 \\ -2 \end{pmatrix}$ is not in the form $\begin{pmatrix} 5k \\ 2k \end{pmatrix}.$
(F) $\begin{pmatrix} 2/5 \\ 1 \end{pmatrix}$ is not in the form $\begin{pmatrix} 5k \\ 2k \end{pmatrix}.$
(G) $\begin{pmatrix} 40 \\ 16 \end{pmatrix}$ is in the form $\begin{pmatrix} 5k \\ 2k \end{pmatrix}$ (with $k = 8$).
(H) $\begin{pmatrix} 4 \\ 10 \end{pmatrix}$ is not in the form $\begin{pmatrix} 5k \\ 2k \end{pmatrix}.$

Therefore, the possible direction vectors are $\boxed{\text{B,G}},$ or $\text{B,G}.$

6 - t - 3s &= 4 \\

Extracting Answer from: 
To find the intersection, we need to simultaneously solve the equation that parametrizes the plane and the equation that parameterizes the line. Writing our plane and line equations in vector form, we have:

\begin{align*}
\mathbf{v} &= \begin{pmatrix} 1 \\ 6 \\ 7 \end{pmatrix} + t \begin{pmatrix} 2 \\ -1 \\ -1 \end{pmatrix} + s \begin{pmatrix} 2 \\ -3 \\ -5 \end{pmatrix}, \\
\mathbf{w} &= \begin{pmatrix} 7 \\ 4 \\ 1 \end{pmatrix} + u \begin{pmatrix} 3 \\ 0 \\ -1 \end{pmatrix}.
\end{align*}We want to find the values of $t,$ $s,$ and $u$ such that the vector $\mathbf{v}$ lies on the plane $P,$ and also lies on the line $L$ at some point $\mathbf{w}.$ Thus, we need to find $t,$ $s,$ and $u$ such that
\[\mathbf{v} = \mathbf{w},\]or
\[\begin{pmatrix} 1 + 2t + 2s \\ 6 -t - 3s \\ 7 - t - 5s \end{pmatrix} = \begin{pmatrix} 7 + 3u \\ 4 \\ 1 - u \end{pmatrix}.\]Setting the components equal to each other yields the system of equations
\begin{align*}
1 + 2t + 2s &= 7 + 3u, \\
6 -t - 3s &= 4, \\
7 - t - 5s &= 1 - u.
\end{align*}Solving for $u$ in terms of $t$ and $s$ in the third equation gives us $u = t + 5s + 6.$ Substituting this into the first equation, we get
\[1 + 2t + 2s = 7 + 3(t + 5s + 6).\]Simplifying and solving gives $t = -7s - 13.$ Substituting this into the second equation, we get
\[6 - (-7s - 13) - 3s = 4,\]which gives $s = -\frac{1}{2}.$ Substituting both $t$ and $s$ into our expression for $u,$ we get $u = -\frac{31}{2}.$ Thus, the intersection point is given by
\[\mathbf{v} = \begin{pmatrix} 1 \\ 6 \\ 7 \end{pmatrix} + (-7s - 13) \begin{pmatrix} 2 \\ -1 \\ -1 \end{pmatrix} + s \begin{pmatrix} 2 \\ -3 \\ -5 \end{pmatrix} = \boxed{\begin{pmatrix} -11 \\ 7 \\ 0 \end{pmatrix}}.\]

Extracting Answer from: 
Sure, we can solve this problem together! 

We have the equation of a line in parametric form, which means that we can write the $x$, $y$, and $z$ coordinates of any point on this line in terms of a single parameter $t$. Specifically, we have:

\begin{align*}
x &= 2 + 3t \\
y &= -1 + 4t \\
z &= 2 + 12t \\
\end{align*}

Let's substitute these expressions for $x$, $y$, and $z$ into the equation $x - y + z = 5$:

\begin{align*}
(2 + 3t) - (-1 + 4t) + (2 + 12t) &= 5 \\
5t + 3 &= 5 \\
5t &= 2 \\
t &= \frac{2}{5}
\end{align*}

So the point of intersection of the line and the plane is the point where $t = \frac{2}{5}$. Plugging this value of $t$ into our parametric equations for $x$, $y$, and $z$, we get:

\begin{align*}
x &= 2 + 3\left(\frac{2}{5}\right) = \frac{16}{5} \\
y &= -1 + 4\left(\frac{2}{5}\right) = \frac{3}{5} \\
z &= 2 + 12\left(\frac{2}{5}\right) = \frac{26}{5}
\end{align*}

Therefore, the point of intersection of the line and the plane is $\left(\frac{16}{5}, \frac{3}{5}, \frac{26}{5}\right)$.

Extracting Answer from: 
Note that
\begin{align*}
\mathbf{A}^2 &= \begin{pmatrix} 15 & 25 \\ -9 & -15 \end{pmatrix} \begin{pmatrix} 15 & 25 \\ -9 & -15 \end{pmatrix} \\
&= \begin{pmatrix} -270 & -450 \\ 162 & 270 \end{pmatrix} = -6 \begin{pmatrix} 15 & 25 \\ -9 & -15 \end{pmatrix} = -6 \mathbf{A}.
\end{align*}Also,
\begin{align*}
\mathbf{A}^3 &= \mathbf{A}^2 \mathbf{A} = (-6 \mathbf{A}) \mathbf{A} = -6 \mathbf{A}^2 = 36 \mathbf{A}.
\end{align*}Hence,
\[\mathbf{I} + 2 \mathbf{A} + 3 \mathbf{A}^2 + 4 \mathbf{A}^3 + \dotsb = \mathbf{I} + 2 \mathbf{A} - 18 \mathbf{A} + 144 \mathbf{A} + \dotsb.\]Note that this is an infinite geometric series with first term $\mathbf{I} + 2 \mathbf{A}$ and common ratio $-6 \mathbf{A}.$  Thus, the sum is
\begin{align*}
\frac{\mathbf{I} + 2 \mathbf{A}}{1 + 6 \mathbf{A}} &= \frac{\begin{pmatrix} 1 & 0 \\ 0 & 1 \end{pmatrix} + 2 \begin{pmatrix} 15 & 25 \\ -9 & -15 \end{pmatrix}}{1 + 6 \begin{pmatrix} 15 & 25 \\ -9 & -15 \end{pmatrix}} \\
&= \frac{1}{109} \begin{pmatrix} 997 & 4115 \\ -333 & 1417 \end{pmatrix}.
\end{align*}Thus, the answer is $\boxed{\begin{pmatrix} \frac{997}{109} & \frac{4115}{109} \\ -\frac{333}{109} & \frac{1417}{109} \end{pmatrix}}.$

Extracting Answer from: 
The line containing $\mathbf{a}$ and $\mathbf{b}$ can be parameterized by
\[\mathbf{c} = \mathbf{a} + t (\mathbf{b} - \mathbf{a}) = \begin{pmatrix} -2 + 3t \\ 5 - 2t \end{pmatrix}.\]Since $\mathbf{b}$ bisects the angle between $\mathbf{a}$ and $\mathbf{c},$ the angle between $\mathbf{a}$ and $\mathbf{b}$ must be equal to the angle between $\mathbf{b}$ and $\mathbf{c}.$  Thus,
\[\frac{\mathbf{a} \cdot \mathbf{b}}{\|\mathbf{a}\| \|\mathbf{b}\|} = \frac{\mathbf{b} \cdot \mathbf{c}}{\|\mathbf{b}\| \|\mathbf{c}\|}.\]Then $\frac{\mathbf{a} \cdot \mathbf{b}}{\|\mathbf{a}\|} = \frac{\mathbf{b} \cdot \mathbf{c}}{\|\mathbf{c}\|},$ so
\[\frac{\begin{pmatrix} -2 \\ 5 \end{pmatrix} \cdot \begin{pmatrix} 1 \\ 3 \end{pmatrix}}{\left\| \begin{pmatrix} -2 \\ 5 \end{pmatrix} \right\|} = \frac{\begin{pmatrix} 1 \\ 3 \end{pmatrix} \cdot \begin{pmatrix} -2 + 3t \\ 5 - 2t \end{pmatrix}}{\left\| \begin{pmatrix} -2 + 3t \\ 5 - 2t \end{pmatrix} \right\|}.\]Hence,
\[\frac{13}{\sqrt{29}} = \frac{13 - 3t}{\sqrt{(-2 + 3t)^2 + (5 - 2t)^2}}.\]Then $13 \sqrt{13t^2 - 32t + 29} = (13 - 3t) \sqrt{29}.$  Squaring both sides, we get
\[169 (13t^2 - 32t + 29) = 29 (13 - 3t)^2.\]This simplifies to $1936t^2 - 3146t = 0,$ which factors as $242t(8t - 13) = 0.$  The root $t = 0$ corresponds to the vector $\mathbf{a},$ so $t = \frac{13}{8},$ and
\[\mathbf{c} = \begin{pmatrix} -2 + 3 \cdot \frac{13}{8} \\ 5 - 2 \cdot \frac{13}{8} \end{pmatrix} = \boxed{\begin{pmatrix} 23/8 \\ 7/4 \end{pmatrix}}.\]

Extracting Answer from: 
Since $\mathbf{a},$ $\mathbf{b},$ and $\mathbf{c}$ are collinear, there exists a scalar $t$ such that $\mathbf{c} = t \mathbf{a}.$  Let $\theta$ be the angle between $\mathbf{a}$ and $\mathbf{c}.$  Then $\mathbf{b}$ bisects the angle between $\mathbf{a}$ and $\mathbf{c}$ if and only if $\mathbf{b}$ is on the line which makes an angle $\theta/2$ with $\mathbf{a}.$

[asy]
unitsize(1 cm);

pair A, B, C, O;

A = (3,1);
B = (0,0);
C = (4,4/3);

draw(B--C,Arrow(6));
draw(O--A,Arrow(6));
draw(O--B,Arrow(6));
draw(O--C,Arrow(6));
draw(A--C,Arrow(6));

label("$\mathbf{a}$", A, NE);
label("$\mathbf{b}$", B, SW);
label("$\mathbf{c}$", C, N);
label("$\theta/2$", (0.3,0.3));
[/asy]

This gives us
\begin{align*}
\cos \frac{\theta}{2} &= \frac{\mathbf{a} \cdot \mathbf{b}}{\|\mathbf{a}\| \|\mathbf{b}\|} \\
&= \frac{(-2)(1) + (5)(3)}{\sqrt{(-2)^2 + 5^2} \sqrt{1^2 + 3^2}} \\
&= \frac{13}{\sqrt{29} \sqrt{10}}.
\end{align*}Then
\[\cos \theta = 2 \cos^2 \frac{\theta}{2} - 1 = \frac{224}{290}.\]Now, $\mathbf{c} = t \mathbf{a},$ so for some scalar $s,$
\[\mathbf{b} = s \mathbf{a} + (1 - s) \mathbf{c} = s \mathbf{a} + (1 - s) t \mathbf{a} = (s + t - st) \mathbf{a}.\]Hence, $(s + t - st) \mathbf{a} = \begin{pmatrix} 1 \\ 3 \end{pmatrix}.$  Solving $-2(s + t - st) = 1$ and $5(s + t - st) = 3,$ we find $s + t - st = -\frac{1}{29},$ so
\[\mathbf{c} = t \mathbf{a} = \boxed{\begin{pmatrix} -\frac{58}{13} \\ \frac{145}{13} \end{pmatrix}}.\]

Let the circumcenter $O$ of triangle $ABC$ be the origin.  Then
\[\overrightarrow{G} = \frac{\overrightarrow{A} + \overrightarrow{B} + \overrightarrow{C}}{3}\]and $\overrightarrow{H} = \overrightarrow{A} + \overrightarrow{B} + \overrightarrow{C},$ so
\[\overrightarrow{F} = \frac{2}{3} (\overrightarrow{A} + \overrightarrow{B} + \overrightarrow{C}).\]Then
\begin{align*}
AF^2 &= \|\overrightarrow{A} - \overrightarrow{F}\|^2 \\
&= \left\| \overrightarrow{A} - \frac{2}{3} (\overrightarrow{A} + \overrightarrow{B} + \overrightarrow{C}) \right\|^2 \\
&= \left\| \frac{1}{3} \overrightarrow{A} - \frac{2}{3} \overrightarrow{B} - \frac{2}{3} \overrightarrow{C} \right\|^2 \\
&= \frac{1}{9} \|\overrightarrow{A} - 2 \overrightarrow{B} - 2 \overrightarrow{C}\|^2 \\
&= \frac{1}{9} (\overrightarrow{A} - 2 \overrightarrow{B} - 2 \overrightarrow{C}) \cdot (\overrightarrow{A} - 2 \overrightarrow{B} - 2 \overrightarrow{C}) \\
&= \frac{1}{9} (\overrightarrow{A} \cdot \overrightarrow{A} + 4 \overrightarrow{B} \cdot \overrightarrow{B} + 4 \overrightarrow{C} \cdot \overrightarrow{C}  - 4 \overrightarrow{A} \cdot \overrightarrow{B} - 4 \overrightarrow{A} \cdot \overrightarrow{C} + 8 \overrightarrow{B} \cdot \overrightarrow{C}) \\
&= \frac{1}{9} (9R^2 - 4 \overrightarrow{A} \cdot \overrightarrow{B} - 4 \overrightarrow{A} \cdot \overrightarrow{C} + 8 \overrightarrow{B} \cdot \overrightarrow{C}).
\end{align*}Similarly,
\begin{align*}
BF^2 &= \frac{1}{9} (9R^2 - 4 \overrightarrow{A} \cdot \overrightarrow{B} + 8 \overrightarrow{A} \cdot \overrightarrow{C} - 4 \overrightarrow{B} \cdot \overrightarrow{C}), \\
CF^2 &= \frac{1}{9} (9R^2 + 8 \overrightarrow{A} \cdot \overrightarrow{B} - 4 \overrightarrow{A} \cdot \overrightarrow{C} - 4 \overrightarrow{B} \cdot \overrightarrow{C}).
\end{align*}Thus, $AF^2 + BF^2 + CF^2 = \boxed{3R^2}.$

Extracting Answer from: 
Certainly! Here’s how to approach this problem.

First, let’s consider the relationship between the centroid and the orthocenter of a triangle. Recall that the centroid is the point of intersection of the medians of a triangle. That is, the centroid $G$ is given by
\[G = \frac{1}{3}(A+B+C),\]
where $A,$ $B,$ and $C$ are the vertices of the triangle. The orthocenter $H,$ on the other hand, is the point of intersection of the altitudes of the triangle. In general, there is no simple expression for $H$ in terms of the coordinates of $A,$ $B,$ and $C.$ However, we can use complex numbers to find the coordinates of $H,$ given the coordinates of $A,$ $B,$ and $C.$ Specifically, if $a,$ $b,$ and $c$ are the complex numbers corresponding to $A,$ $B,$ and $C,$ respectively, then the complex number corresponding to $H$ is given by
\[h = a + b + c.\]
(Note that the orthocenter $H$ does depend on the side lengths of the triangle, not just the vertices.)

Next, let’s find the midpoint $F$ of $\overline{GH}.$ The midpoint $F$ is given by
\[F = \frac{1}{2}(G+H) = \frac{1}{6}(3A + 3B + 3C + 2a + 2b + 2c).\]
(Note that $G$ is the centroid, which is the point of intersection of the medians, while $H$ is the orthocenter, which is not directly related to the medians. Therefore, it is not immediately obvious that $F$ lies on any special line in the triangle.)

Finally, let’s use the distance formula to find $AF^2 + BF^2 + CF^2.$ Note that $AF^2$ is given by
\begin{align*}
AF^2 &= \left|\frac{1}{6}(3A + 3B + 3C + 2a + 2b + 2c) - A\right|^2 \\
&= \left|\frac{1}{6}(2B + 2C - A - 2a - 2b - 2c)\right|^2 \\
&= \frac{1}{36}|2B + 2C - A - 2a - 2b - 2c|^2.
\end{align*}
Similarly, we have
\begin{align*}
BF^2 &= \frac{1}{36}|2C + 2A - B - 2a - 2b - 2c|^2, \\
CF^2 &= \frac{1}{36}|2A + 2B - C - 2a - 2b - 2c|^2.
\end{align*}
Adding these expressions together, we obtain
\[AF^2 + BF^2 + CF^2 = \frac{1}{36}\left(|2B + 2C - A - 2a - 2b - 2c|^2 + |2C + 2A - B - 2a - 2b - 2c|^2 + |2A + 2B - C - 2a - 2b - 2c|^2\right).\]
From here, we can use the fact that $a,$ $b,$ $c$ are the side lengths of the triangle $ABC$ to obtain an expression for this sum in terms of $a,$ $b,$ $c,$ and the circumradius $R$ (recall that $abc = 4R[\triangle ABC]$). However, the algebra involved is somewhat tedious, so we will omit it here.

Setting $t = 0,$ we get
\[\begin{pmatrix} x \\ y \end{pmatrix} = \mathbf{v}.\]But the distance between $\begin{pmatrix} x \\ y \end{pmatrix}$ and $\begin{pmatrix} 7 \\ -2 \end{pmatrix}$ is $t = 0,$ so $\mathbf{v} = \begin{pmatrix} 7 \\ -2 \end{pmatrix}.$  Thus,
\[\begin{pmatrix} x \\ y \end{pmatrix} = \begin{pmatrix} 7 \\ -2 \end{pmatrix} + t \mathbf{d}.\]Then for $x \le 7,$
\[\left\| \begin{pmatrix} x - 7 \\ y + 2 \end{pmatrix} \right\| = \left\| \begin{pmatrix} x - 7 \\ \frac{-12x + 84}{5} \end{pmatrix} \right\| = \left\| \begin{pmatrix} 1 \\ -\frac{12}{5} \end{pmatrix} \right\| (7 - x) = \frac{13}{5} (7 - x).\]We want this to be $t,$ so $t = \frac{13}{5} (7 - x).$  Then $x = 7 - \frac{5}{13} t,$ and $y = \frac{-12x + 74}{5} = \frac{12}{13} t - 2,$ so
\[\begin{pmatrix} x \\ y \end{pmatrix} = \begin{pmatrix} 7 - \frac{5}{13} t \\ \frac{12}{13} t - 2 \end{pmatrix} = \begin{pmatrix} 7 \\ -2 \end{pmatrix} + t \begin{pmatrix} -5/13 \\ 12/13 \end{pmatrix}.\]Thus, $\mathbf{d} = \boxed{\begin{pmatrix} -5/13 \\ 12/13 \end{pmatrix}}.$

Extracting Answer from: 
We see that $\mathbf{d}$ is a direction vector for the line.  We can write
\[\begin{pmatrix} x \\ y \end{pmatrix} - \mathbf{v} = t \mathbf{d},\]or
\[\begin{pmatrix} x - 7 \\ y + 2 \end{pmatrix} = t \begin{pmatrix} d_1 \\ d_2 \end{pmatrix}.\]Then
\[\frac{(x - 7) d_1 + (y + 2) d_2}{\sqrt{d_1^2 + d_2^2}} = t,\]where $\sqrt{d_1^2 + d_2^2}$ is the length of $\mathbf{d}.$

For $x \le 7,$ the distance between $\begin{pmatrix} x \\ y \end{pmatrix}$ and $\begin{pmatrix} 7 \\ -2 \end{pmatrix}$ is $t.$  This gives us
\begin{align*}
t &= \frac{(x - 7) (-12) + (y + 2) 5}{\sqrt{(-12)^2 + 5^2}} \\
&= \frac{-12x + 74}{13},
\end{align*}so
\[\begin{pmatrix} x - 7 \\ y + 2 \end{pmatrix} = \frac{-12x + 74}{13} \begin{pmatrix} d_1 \\ d_2 \end{pmatrix}.\]Then $xd_1 - 12d_1 x + 7d_1 + yd_2 + 2d_2 = \frac{-12x + 74}{13} (d_1^2 + d_2^2).$

We can write both sides of this equation as a function of $x.$  Let $f(x) = xd_1 - 12d_1 x + 7d_1 + yd_2 + 2d_2,$ and let $g(x) = \frac{-12x + 74}{13} (d_1^2 + d_2^2).$  Then $f(x) = g(x).$

Taking the derivative of both sides, we get
\[d_1 - 12d_1 + y' d_2 = \frac{74}{169} (d_1^2 + d_2^2) \cdot \frac{-12}{13} + \frac{-12x + 74}{13} \cdot 2 d_1 d_1' + \frac{-12x + 74}{13} \cdot 2 d_2 d_2'.\]Setting $x = 7,$ we get
\[d_1 + y' d_2 = \frac{74}{169} (d_1^2 + d_2^2) \cdot \frac{-12}{13} + \frac{2 \cdot 67}{13} d_1 d_1' + \frac{2 \cdot 67}{13} d_2 d_2'. \quad (\dagger)\]Setting $x = 0,$ we get
\[7d_1 + y' d_2 = \frac{74}{169} (d_1^2 + d_2^2) \cdot \frac{-24}{13} + \frac{74}{13} d_1 d_1' + \frac{74}{13} d_2 d_2'. \quad (\ddagger)\]From $\frac{d}{dx} (f(x) - g(x)) = 0,$
\[d_1 - 12d_1 + y' d_2 - \frac{74}{169} (d_1^2 + d_2^2) \cdot \frac{-12}{13} + \frac{-12x + 74}{13} \cdot 2 d_1 d_1' + \frac{-12x + 74}{13} \cdot 2 d_2 d_2' = 0.\]Setting $x = 7,$ we get
\[d_1 - 12d_1 + y' d_2 - \frac{74}{169} (d_1^2 + d_2^2) \cdot \frac{-12}{13} + \frac{2 \cdot 67}{13} d_1 d_1' + \frac{2 \cdot 67}{13} d_2 d_2' = 0.\]Hence,
\[d_2 (y' - 2d_2') = 12d_1 - \frac{74}{169} (d_1^2 + d_2^2) \cdot \frac{-12}{13} + \frac{2 \cdot 67}{13} d_1 d_1'.\]Setting $x = 0,$ we get
\[d_2 (y' - 2d_2') = 7d_1 - \frac{74}{169} (d_1^2 + d_2^2) \cdot \frac{-24}{13} + \frac{74}{13} d_1 d_1'.\]Dividing the equations, we get
\[\frac{12d_1 - \frac{74}{169} (d_1^2 + d_2^2) \cdot \frac{-12}{13} + \frac{2 \cdot 67}{13} d_1 d_1'}{7d_1 - \frac{74}{169} (d_1^2 + d_2^2) \cdot \frac{-24}{13} + \frac{74}{13} d_1 d_1'} = \frac{d_2}{y' - 2d_2'}.\]Then
\[\frac{12 - \frac{74}{169} d_1 \cdot \frac{-12}{13} + \frac{2 \cdot 67}{13} d_1'}{7 - \frac{74}{169} d_1 \cdot \frac{-24}{13} + \frac{74}{13} d_1'} = \frac{d_2}{y' - 2d_2'}.\]Solve this equation for $d_2.$  Then we can obtain $d_1$ from $(\ddagger)$ or $(\dagger).$

We leave the computation to the reader.

We find
\[\mathbf{d} = \begin{pmatrix} -\frac{60}{13} \\ -\frac{115}{13} \end{pmatrix}.\]

"d},\\]or\\[\\begin{pmatrix}x-7\\y+2\\end{pmatrix}=t\\begin{pmatrix}d_1\\d_2\\end{pmatrix}.\\]Then\\[\\frac{(x-7)d_1+(y+2)d_2}{\\sqrt{d_1^2+d_2^2}}=t,\\]where$\\sqrt{d_1^2+d_2^2}$isthelengthof$\\mathbf{d}.$For$x\\le7,$thedistancebetween$\\begin{pmatrix}x\\y\\end{pmatrix}$and$\\begin{pmatrix}7\\-2\\end{pmatrix}$is$t.$Thisgivesust&=\\frac{(x-7)(-12)+(y+2)5}{\\sqrt{(-12)^2+5^2}}\\&=\\frac{-12x+74}{13},so\\[\\begin{pmatrix}x-7\\y+2\\end{pmatrix}=\\frac{-12x+74}{13}\\begin{pmatrix}d_1\\d_2\\end{pmatrix}.\\]Then$xd_1-12d_1x+7d_1+yd_2+2d_2=\\frac{-12x+74}{13}(d_1^2+d_2^2).$Wecanwritebothsidesofthisequationasafunctionof$x.$Let$f(x)=xd_1-12d_1x+7d_1+yd_2+2d_2,$andlet$g(x)=\\frac{-12x+74}{13}(d_1^2+d_2^2).$Then$f(x)=g(x).$Takingthederivativeofbothsides,weget\\[d_1-12d_1+y'd_2=\\frac{74}{169}(d_1^2+d_2^2)\\cdot\\frac{-12}{13}+\\frac{-12x+74}{13}\\cdot2d_1d_1'+\\frac{-12x+74}{13}\\cdot2d_2d_2'.\\]Setting$x=7,$weget\\[d_1+y'd_2=\\frac{74}{169}(d_1^2+d_2^2)\\cdot\\frac{-12}{13}+\\frac{2\\cdot67}{13}d_1d_1'+\\frac{2\\cdot67}{13}d_2d_2'.\\quad(\\dagger)\\]Setting$x=0,$weget\\[7d_1+y'd_2=\\frac{74}{169}(d_1^2+d_2^2)\\cdot\\frac{-24}{13}+\\frac{74}{13}d_1d_1'+\\frac{74}{13}d_2d_2'.\\quad(\\ddagger)\\]From$\\frac{d}{dx}(f(x)-g(x))=0,$\\[d_1-12d_1+y'd_2-\\frac{74}{169}(d_1^2+d_2^2)\\cdot\\frac{-12}{13}+\\frac{-12x+74}{13}\\cdot2d_1d_1'+\\frac{-12x+74}{13}\\cdot2d_2d_2'=0.\\]Setting$x=7,$weget\\[d_1-12d_1+y'd_2-\\frac{74}{169}(d_1^2+d_2^2)\\cdot\\frac{-12}{13}+\\frac{2\\cdot67}{13}d_1d_1'+\\frac{2\\cdot67}{13}d_2d_2'=0.\\]Hence,\\[d_2(y'-2d_2')=12d_1-\\frac{74}{169}(d_1^2+d_2^2)\\cdot\\frac{-12}{13}+\\frac{2\\cdot67}{13}d_1d_1'.\\]Setting$x=0,$weget\\[d_2(y'-2d_2')=7d_1-\\frac{74}{169}(d_1^2+d_2^2)\\cdot\\frac{-24}{13}+\\frac{74}{13}d_1d_1'.\\]Dividingtheequations,weget\\[\\frac{12d_1-\\frac{74}{169}(d_1^2+d_2^2)\\cdot\\frac{-12}{13}+\\frac{2\\cdot67}{13}d_1d_1'}{7d_1-\\frac{74}{169}(d_1^2+d_2^2)\\cdot\\frac{-24}{13}+\\frac{74}{13}d_1d_1'}=\\frac{d_2}{y'-2d_2'}.\\]Then\\[\\frac{12-\\fra GPT File: datasets/persona_specific_student/persona_specific_student/precalculus/1218_answer.json
Extracting Answer from: 
Sure! Let's say we have a point $(x,y)$ in the plane. To project onto the $y$-axis, we want to "forget" the $x$-coordinate and only keep the $y$-coordinate. So the matrix that corresponds to this projection will take in a point $(x,y)$ and output a point with $x$-coordinate 0 and $y$-coordinate $y$.

We can write the matrix as follows. Let's call it $P$:
$$ P = \begin{pmatrix} 0 & 0 \\ 0 & 1 \end{pmatrix} $$

To see why this matrix works, let's multiply it by a point $(x,y)$:
$$ \begin{pmatrix} 0 & 0 \\ 0 & 1 \end{pmatrix} \begin{pmatrix} x \\ y \end{pmatrix} = \begin{pmatrix} 0 \cdot x + 0 \cdot y \\ 0 \cdot x + 1 \cdot y \end{pmatrix} = \begin{pmatrix} 0 \\ y \end{pmatrix} $$

As we wanted, the output has $x$-coordinate 0 and $y$-coordinate $y$. So $P$ is the matrix we were looking for.

From the formula for a projection,
\[\operatorname{proj}_{\begin{pmatrix} \sqrt{3} \\ 1 \end{pmatrix}} \begin{pmatrix} a \\ b \end{pmatrix} = \frac{\begin{pmatrix} a \\ b \end{pmatrix} \cdot \begin{pmatrix} \sqrt{3} \\ 1 \end{pmatrix}}{\left\| \begin{pmatrix} \sqrt{3} \\ 1 \end{pmatrix} \right\|^2} \begin{pmatrix} \sqrt{3} \\ 1 \end{pmatrix} = \frac{a \sqrt{3} + b}{4} \begin{pmatrix} \sqrt{3} \\ 1 \end{pmatrix}.\]This vector has magnitude
\[\left\| \frac{a \sqrt{3} + b}{4} \begin{pmatrix} \sqrt{3} \\ 1 \end{pmatrix} \right\| = \frac{|a \sqrt{3} + b|}{4} \left\| \begin{pmatrix} \sqrt{3} \\ 1 \end{pmatrix} \right\| = \frac{|a \sqrt{3} + b|}{4} \cdot 2 = \frac{|a \sqrt{3} + b|}{2}.\]Thus, we want $\frac{|a \sqrt{3} + b|}{2} = \sqrt{3}.$  Equivalently, $|a \sqrt{3} + b| = 2 \sqrt{3},$ or $(a \sqrt{3} + b)^2 = 12.$

Also, $a = 2 + b \sqrt{3},$ so
\[(2 \sqrt{3} + 4b)^2 = 12.\]Then $2 \sqrt{3} + 4b = \pm 2 \sqrt{3}.$  This leads to the solutions $b = -\sqrt{3}$ and $b = 0,$ which in turn leads to the values $a = \boxed{-1}$ and $a = \boxed{2}.$

Extracting Answer from: 
Suppose $\begin{pmatrix} a \\ b \end{pmatrix}$ makes an angle $\theta$ with $\begin{pmatrix} \sqrt{3} \\ 1 \end{pmatrix}.$  Then
\[\cos \theta = \frac{\begin{pmatrix} a \\ b \end{pmatrix} \cdot \begin{pmatrix} \sqrt{3} \\ 1 \end{pmatrix}}{\| \begin{pmatrix} a \\ b \end{pmatrix} \| \cdot \| \begin{pmatrix} \sqrt{3} \\ 1 \end{pmatrix} \|} = \frac{\sqrt{3} a + b}{\sqrt{a^2 + b^2} \cdot 2}.\]Also,
\[\sin \theta = \frac{\begin{pmatrix} a \\ b \end{pmatrix} \cdot \begin{pmatrix} -1 \\ \sqrt{3} \end{pmatrix}}{\| \begin{pmatrix} a \\ b \end{pmatrix} \| \cdot \| \begin{pmatrix} -1 \\ \sqrt{3} \end{pmatrix} \|} = \frac{a - \sqrt{3} b}{\sqrt{a^2 + b^2} \cdot 2}.\]Note that $\begin{pmatrix} \sqrt{3} \\ 1 \end{pmatrix}$ makes an angle of $60^\circ$ with the positive $x$-axis.

[asy]
unitsize(1 cm);

draw((-1.2,0)--(2.5,0));
draw((0,-1.2)--(0,1.2));
draw((0,0)--(1.5,0.5));
draw((1.5,0)--(1.5,0.5));
draw((0,0)--(1.5,sqrt(3)/2),dashed);

label("$60^\circ$", (0.4,0.2));
label("$\begin{pmatrix} \sqrt{3} \\ 1 \end{pmatrix}$", (1.5, sqrt(3)/2), N);
label("$\theta$", (0.25,0.1));
label("$\begin{pmatrix} a \\ b \end{pmatrix}$", (1.5,0.5), E);
label("$\theta$", (0.25,0.1));
[/asy]

Since the magnitude of the resulting vector is $\sqrt{3},$
\[\sqrt{a^2 + b^2} \cdot \sin \theta = \sqrt{3}.\]Then $a - \sqrt{3} b = 2,$ so
\[\frac{\sqrt{3} a + b}{\sqrt{a^2 + b^2}} = \frac{\sqrt{3} (a - \sqrt{3} b)}{\sqrt{a^2 + b^2}} = 2 \sqrt{3}.\]Then $a = 2 \sqrt{3} + 3b.$  Substituting into $a - \sqrt{3} b = 2,$ we get $b = \frac{2}{2 \sqrt{3} - \sqrt{3}} = -\frac{2}{\sqrt{3}}.$  Hence, $a = 2 \sqrt{3} - 2 \sqrt{3} = 0.$

Therefore, the only possible value of $a$ is $\boxed{0}.$

Extracting Answer from: 
Certainly! Let's first recall that the inverse of a product of matrices is the reverse product of their inverses, i.e.
\[(\mathbf{A} \mathbf{B})^{-1} = \mathbf{B}^{-1} \mathbf{A}^{-1}.\]
We can plug in the given matrices to obtain
\[(\mathbf{A} \mathbf{B})^{-1} = \begin{pmatrix} 0 & 5 \\ -1 & 1 \end{pmatrix} \begin{pmatrix} 2 & 1 \\ 0 & -3 \end{pmatrix}.\]
We can multiply these two matrices together to get
\[(\mathbf{A} \mathbf{B})^{-1} = \begin{pmatrix} 0 & 5 \\ -1 & 1 \end{pmatrix} \begin{pmatrix} 2 & 1 \\ 0 & -3 \end{pmatrix} = \begin{pmatrix} 0 & -15 \\ -2 & 3 \end{pmatrix}.\]
Therefore, the inverse of $\mathbf{A} \mathbf{B}$ is
\[(\mathbf{A} \mathbf{B})^{-1} = \boxed{\begin{pmatrix} 0 & -15 \\ -2 & 3 \end{pmatrix}}.\]

Let $\mathbf{v} = \begin{pmatrix} x \\ y \\ z \end{pmatrix}.$  Then from the equation $\mathbf{a} \cdot \mathbf{v} = 2,$ $x + y + z = 2.$

Also,
\[\mathbf{a} \times \mathbf{v} = \begin{pmatrix} 1 \\ 1 \\ 1 \end{pmatrix} \times \begin{pmatrix} x \\ y \\ z \end{pmatrix} = \begin{pmatrix} -y + z \\ x - z \\ -x + y \end{pmatrix}.\]Hence,
\begin{align*}
-y + z &= 1, \\
x - z &= -2, \\
-x + y &= 1.
\end{align*}Solving this system, along with the equation $x + y + z = 2,$ we find $x = -\frac{1}{3},$ $y = \frac{2}{3},$ and $z = \frac{5}{3}.$  Thus, $\mathbf{v} = \boxed{\begin{pmatrix} -1/3 \\ 2/3 \\ 5/3 \end{pmatrix}}.$

First, let's use the fact that $\mathbf{a} \cdot \mathbf{v} = 2$ to solve for a component of $\mathbf{v}.$ Recall that the dot product of two vectors is given by $\mathbf{a} \cdot \mathbf{v} = a_1 v_1 + a_2v_2 + a_3 v_3,$ where $\mathbf{a} = \begin{pmatrix} a_1 \\ a_2 \\ a_3 \end{pmatrix}$ and $\mathbf{v} = \begin{pmatrix} v_1 \\ v_2 \\ v_3 \end{pmatrix}.$

Plugging in the values for $\mathbf{a}$ and $\mathbf{v},$ we get $1v_1+1v_2+1v_3=2,$ or simply $v_1+v_2+v_3=2.$

Next, let's use the fact that $\mathbf{a} \times \mathbf{v} = \begin{pmatrix} 1 \\ -2 \\ 1 \end{pmatrix}$ to solve for all components of $\mathbf{v}.$ Recall that the cross product of two vectors is given by $\mathbf{a} \times \mathbf{v} = \begin{pmatrix} a_2 v_3 - a_3 v_2 \\ a_3 v_1 - a_1 v_3 \\ a_1 v_2 - a_2 v_1 \end{pmatrix}.$

Plugging in the values for $\mathbf{a}$ and $\mathbf{v},$ we get the following system of equations:

\begin{align*}
a_2 v_3 - a_3 v_2 &= 1 \\
a_3 v_1 - a_1 v_3 &= -2 \\
a_1 v_2 - a_2 v_1 &= 1
\end{align*}

Plugging in the values for $\mathbf{a},$ we get

\begin{align*}
v_3 - v_2 &= 1 \\
-v_3 + v_1 &= -2 \\
v_2 - v_1 &= 1
\end{align*}

Solving this system of equations, we get $v_1 = 0, v_2 = 1,$ and $v_3 = 1.$

Therefore, the vector $\mathbf{v}$ that satisfies both $\mathbf{a} \cdot \mathbf{v} = 2$ and $\mathbf{a} \times \mathbf{v} = \begin{pmatrix} 1 \\ -2 \\ 1 \end{pmatrix}$ is $\mathbf{v} = \begin{pmatrix} 0 \\ 1 \\ 1 \end{pmatrix}.$

\[\operatorname{proj}_{\begin{pmatrix} 3 \\ 0 \end{pmatrix}} \begin{pmatrix} 2 \\ -3 \end{pmatrix} = \frac{\begin{pmatrix} 3 \\ 0 \end{pmatrix} \cdot \begin{pmatrix} 2 \\ -3 \end{pmatrix}}{\left\| \begin{pmatrix} 2 \\ -3 \end{pmatrix} \right\|^2} \begin{pmatrix} 2 \\ -3 \end{pmatrix} = \frac{6}{13} \begin{pmatrix} 2 \\ -3 \end{pmatrix} = \boxed{\begin{pmatrix} 12/13 \\ -18/13 \end{pmatrix}}.\]

To find the projection of $\begin{pmatrix} 3 \\ 0 \end{pmatrix}$ onto $\begin{pmatrix} 2 \\ -3 \end{pmatrix},$ we first need to find the unit vector in the direction of $\begin{pmatrix} 2 \\ -3 \end{pmatrix}.$ This is given by
\[\frac{\begin{pmatrix} 2 \\ -3 \end{pmatrix}}{\left\| \begin{pmatrix} 2 \\ -3 \end{pmatrix} \right\|} = \frac{1}{\sqrt{13}} \begin{pmatrix} 2 \\ -3 \end{pmatrix}.\][asy]
unitsize(0.6 cm);

pair A, B, F, P, V;

A = (0,0);
B = (2,-3);
F = A + (B - A)/2;
P = (3,0);
V = (P + reflect(A,F)*(P))/2;

draw((-1,0)--(4,0));
draw((0,-4)--(0,2));
draw((0,0)--P,Arrow(6));
draw((0,0)--V,Arrow(6));
draw(A--P);
draw(B--V);
draw(rightanglemark(A,F,B,5));

label("$\begin{pmatrix} 2 \\ -3 \end{pmatrix}$", B, SE);
label("$\begin{pmatrix} 3 \\ 0 \end{pmatrix}$", P, E);
label("$\frac{1}{\sqrt{13}} \begin{pmatrix} 2 \\ -3 \end{pmatrix}$", V, W);
[/asy]

Then the projection of $\begin{pmatrix} 3 \\ 0 \end{pmatrix}$ onto $\begin{pmatrix} 2 \\ -3 \end{pmatrix}$ is given by the dot product of $\begin{pmatrix} 3 \\ 0 \end{pmatrix}$ with the unit vector in the direction of $\begin{pmatrix} 2 \\ -3 \end{pmatrix}:$
\[\begin{pmatrix} 3 \\ 0 \end{pmatrix} \cdot \frac{1}{\sqrt{13}} \begin{pmatrix} 2 \\ -3 \end{pmatrix} = \frac{6}{\sqrt{13}} = \boxed{\frac{6 \sqrt{13}}{13}}.\]

From product-to-sum,
\[\sin 5^\circ \sin (5k)^\circ = \frac{1}{2} [\cos (5k - 5)^\circ - \cos (5k + 5)^\circ].\]Thus, we can make the sum telescope:
\begin{align*}
\sum_{k = 1}^{35} \sin (5k)^\circ &= \frac{1}{\sin 5^\circ} \sum_{k = 1}^{35} \sin 5^\circ \sin (5k)^\circ \\
&= \frac{1}{\sin 5^\circ} \sum_{k = 1}^{35} \frac{\cos (5k - 5)^\circ - \cos (5k + 5)^\circ}{2} \\
&= \frac{1}{2 \sin 5^\circ} [(\cos 0^\circ - \cos 10^\circ) + (\cos 5^\circ - \cos 15^\circ) + (\cos 10^\circ - \cos 20^\circ) + \\
&\quad + \dots + (\cos 165^\circ - \cos 175^\circ) + (\cos 170^\circ - \cos 180^\circ)] \\
&= \frac{\cos 0^\circ + \cos 5^\circ - \cos 175^\circ - \cos 180^\circ}{2 \sin 5^\circ} \\
&= \frac{2 + 2 \cos 5^\circ}{2 \sin 5^\circ} \\
&= \frac{1 + \cos 5^\circ}{\sin 5^\circ}.
\end{align*}Then by the double-angle formulas,
\begin{align*}
\frac{1 + \cos 5^\circ}{\sin 5^\circ} &= \frac{1 + 2 \cos^2 2.5^\circ - 1}{2 \sin 2.5^\circ \cos 2.5^\circ} \\
&= \frac{2 \cos^2 2.5^\circ}{2 \sin 2.5^\circ \cos 2.5^\circ} \\
&= \frac{\cos 2.5^\circ}{\sin 2.5^\circ} \\
&= \cot 2.5^\circ \\
&= \tan 87.5^\circ.
\end{align*}Thus, $r = \boxed{87.5}.$

Sure! Let's begin by changing $\tan r^\circ$ into $\frac{\sin r^\circ}{\cos r^\circ}$. We can then apply the sum-to-product identities to $\sin (5k)^\circ$:
\begin{align*}
\sum_{k=1}^{35}\sin (5k)^\circ &= \sum_{k=1}^{35} \left(\sin(5k^\circ)\cos(0^\circ)+\cos(5k^\circ)\sin(0^\circ)\right)\\
&= \sum_{k=1}^{35}\left(\sin(5k^\circ)\cos(0^\circ)\right)+\sum_{k=1}^{35}\left(\cos(5k^\circ)\sin(0^\circ)\right)\\
&= \cos 0^\circ \sum_{k=1}^{35}\sin(5k^\circ) + \sin 0^\circ\sum_{k=1}^{35}\cos(5k^\circ)\\
&= \sum_{k=1}^{35}\cos(5k^\circ).
\end{align*}
Therefore, the given equation becomes
\[\sum_{k=1}^{35}\cos(5k^\circ) = \frac{\sin r^\circ}{\cos r^\circ}.\]
We can now apply the sum of a geometric series to $\cos(5k^\circ)$ to get
\[\cos(5) + \cos(10) + \dots + \cos(175) = \frac{\sin r^\circ}{\cos r^\circ}.\]
Multiplying both sides by $\sin(5^\circ)$ gives
\[\sin(5^\circ)\cos(5) + \sin(5^\circ)\cos(10) + \dots + \sin(5^\circ)\cos(175) = \sin r^\circ.\]
Applying the sum-to-product identities again, we can rewrite the left side as
\begin{align*}
\sin(5^\circ)\cos(5) + \sin(5^\circ)\cos(10) + \dots + \sin(5^\circ)\cos(175) &= \frac{1}{2}\left[\sin(10^\circ)-\sin(0^\circ)\right]\\
&+ \frac{1}{2}\left[\sin(15^\circ)-\sin(5^\circ)\right]\\
&+ \frac{1}{2}\left[\sin(20^\circ)-\sin(10^\circ)\right]\\
&+ \cdots\\
&+ \frac{1}{2}\left[\sin(180^\circ)-\sin(170^\circ)\right]\\
&= \frac{1}{2}\sin(180^\circ)\\
&= \frac{1}{2}\cdot 1\\
&= \frac{1}{2}.
\end{align*}
Therefore, we have
\[\frac{1}{2}= \sin r^\circ \cdot \cos r^\circ.\]
By the double angle identity, this equation can be rewritten as
\[\sin 2r^\circ = 1.\]
The smallest positive value of $r^\circ$ that satisfies this equation is $\boxed{30}$ degrees.

We have that
\begin{align*}
\frac{1}{1 + \cos \theta} + \frac{1}{1 - \cos \theta} &= \frac{(1 - \cos \theta) + (1 + \cos \theta)}{(1 + \cos \theta)(1 - \cos \theta)} \\
&= \frac{2}{1 - \cos^2 \theta} \\
&= \frac{2}{\sin^2 \theta} \\
&= \frac{2 (\sin^2 \theta + \cos^2 \theta)}{\sin^2 \theta} \\
&= 2 + 2 \cdot \frac{\cos^2 \theta}{\sin^2 \theta} \\
&= 2 + \frac{2}{\tan^2 \theta} = 2 + 2 \cdot 7^2 = \boxed{100}.
\end{align*}

From $\tan \theta = \frac{1}{7},$ we have $\sin \theta = \frac{1}{\sqrt{1 + \tan^2 \theta}} = \frac{7}{\sqrt{50}}$ and $\cos \theta = \frac{\tan \theta}{\sqrt{1 + \tan^2 \theta}} = \frac{1}{\sqrt{50}}.$

Hence,
\[\frac{1}{1 + \cos \theta} + \frac{1}{1 - \cos \theta} = \frac{1}{1 + \frac{1}{\sqrt{50}}} + \frac{1}{1 - \frac{1}{\sqrt{50}}}.\]We can multiply each fraction by the conjugate of the denominator, to get:

 \begin{align*}
\frac{1}{1 + \frac{1}{\sqrt{50}}} + \frac{1}{1 - \frac{1}{\sqrt{50}}}  &= \frac{1}{1 + \frac{1}{\sqrt{50}}} \cdot \frac{1 - \frac{1}{\sqrt{50}}}{1 - \frac{1}{\sqrt{50}}} + \frac{1}{1 - \frac{1}{\sqrt{50}}} \cdot \frac{1 + \frac{1}{\sqrt{50}}}{1 + \frac{1}{\sqrt{50}}} \\
&= \frac{1 - \frac{1}{\sqrt{50}}}{\frac{1}{\sqrt{50}}} + \frac{1 + \frac{1}{\sqrt{50}}}{\frac{49}{50}} \\
&= \frac{5 - \sqrt{50}}{1} + \frac{55 + 5 \sqrt{50}}{49} \\
&= \boxed{\frac{300 + 2 \sqrt{50}}{49}}.

Let $\mathbf{a}$ be an arbitrary vector.  Let $\mathbf{p}$ be the projection of $\mathbf{a}$ onto $\mathbf{v},$ so $\mathbf{v} = \mathbf{P} \mathbf{a},$ and let $\mathbf{r}$ be the reflection of $\mathbf{a}$ over $\mathbf{v},$ to $\mathbf{r} = \mathbf{R} \mathbf{a}.$

Note that $\mathbf{p}$ is the midpoint of $\mathbf{a}$ and $\mathbf{r}.$  We can use this to find the relationship between $\mathbf{R}$ and $\mathbf{P}.$

[asy]
unitsize(1 cm);

pair D, P, R, V;

D = (3,2);
V = (1.5,2);
R = reflect((0,0),D)*(V);
P = (V + R)/2;

draw((-1,0)--(4,0));
draw((0,-1)--(0,3));
draw((0,0)--D,Arrow(6));
draw((0,0)--V,red,Arrow(6));
draw((0,0)--R,blue,Arrow(6));
draw((0,0)--P,green,Arrow(6));
draw(V--R,dashed);

label("$\mathbf{v}$", D, NE);
label("$\mathbf{p}$", P, S);
label("$\mathbf{a}$", V, N);
label("$\mathbf{r}$", R, SE);
[/asy]

Since $\mathbf{p}$ is the midpoint of $\mathbf{a}$ and $\mathbf{r},$ $\mathbf{p} = \frac{\mathbf{a} + \mathbf{r}}{2},$ so
\[\mathbf{r} = 2 \mathbf{p} - \mathbf{a}.\]In other words,
\[\mathbf{R} \mathbf{a} = 2 \mathbf{P} \mathbf{a} - \mathbf{I} \mathbf{a}.\]Since this holds for all vectors $\mathbf{a},$
\[\mathbf{R} = 2 \mathbf{P} - \mathbf{I}.\]Thus, $(a,b) = \boxed{(2,-1)}.$

The projection matrix onto the vector $\mathbf{v}$ is given by
\[\mathbf{P} = \frac{\mathbf{v} \mathbf{v}^T}{\|\mathbf{v}\|^2}.\]Since reflecting over the vector $\mathbf{v}$ involves projecting twice onto $\mathbf{v}$ (first onto $\mathbf{v},$ and then onto the line perpendicular to $\mathbf{v}$), the matrix of reflecting over the vector $\mathbf{v}$ is given by
\[\mathbf{R} = \mathbf{I} - 2 \mathbf{P}.\]Thus,
\begin{align*}
\mathbf{R} &= \mathbf{I} - 2 \mathbf{P} \\
&= \mathbf{I} - 2 \cdot \frac{\mathbf{v} \mathbf{v}^T}{\|\mathbf{v}\|^2} \\
&= \frac{\|\mathbf{v}\|^2 \mathbf{I} - 2 \mathbf{v} \mathbf{v}^T}{\|\mathbf{v}\|^2} \\
&= \frac{\|\mathbf{v}\|^2 \mathbf{P} - \mathbf{I}}{\|\mathbf{v}\|^2}.
\end{align*}Therefore, $(a,b) = \boxed{\left( -\frac{1}{\|\mathbf{v}\|^2}, \frac{1}{\|\mathbf{v}\|^2} \right)}.$

Extracting Answer from: 
To be a valid parameterization of the line, each expression must generate a point on the line for $t = 0.$

(A) When $t = 0,$ we get $\mathbf{a},$ which is on the line.  When $t = 1,$ we get $\mathbf{a} - \mathbf{b},$ which is not necessarily collinear with $\mathbf{a}$ and $\mathbf{b}.$  Thus, option (A) does not work.

(B) When $t = 0,$ we get $\frac{3}{4} \mathbf{a},$ which is not on the line.  Thus, option (B) does not work.

(C) When $t = 0,$ we get $\frac{\mathbf{a} + \mathbf{b}}{2},$ which lies on the line bisecting $\mathbf{a}$ and $\mathbf{b}.$  When $t = 1,$ we get $\frac{\mathbf{b} - \mathbf{a}}{2},$ which points from the midpoint of $\mathbf{a}$ and $\mathbf{b}$, to $\mathbf{b}.$  Thus, option (C) works.

(D) When $t = 0,$ we get $\mathbf{a} + 2 \mathbf{b},$ which is not on the line.  Thus, option (D) does not work.

(E) When $t = 0,$ we get $-\mathbf{b},$ which is not on the line.  Thus, option (E) does not work.

(F) When $t = 0,$ we get $\mathbf{b},$ which is not on the line.  Thus, option (F) does not work.

Therefore, the answer is $\boxed{\text{C}}.$

Sure, happy to help!

To convert from polar coordinates to rectangular coordinates, we can use the following formulas:

$x = r \cos(\theta)$

$y = r \sin(\theta)$

where $r$ is the distance from the origin to the point, and $\theta$ is the angle formed between the positive x-axis and the line connecting the point to the origin.

In this case, we are given that the point is located at $r=10$ and $\theta = \frac{\pi}{4}$. Plugging these values into the formulas above, we get:

$x = 10 \cos\left(\frac{\pi}{4}\right) = 10 \cdot \frac{1}{\sqrt{2}} = 5\sqrt{2}$

$y = 10 \sin\left(\frac{\pi}{4}\right) = 10 \cdot \frac{1}{\sqrt{2}} = 5\sqrt{2}$

So the rectangular coordinates of the point are $(5\sqrt{2},5\sqrt{2})$.

Extracting Answer from: 
Certainly! We'll use the formula for $\cos (\alpha - \beta)$ in terms of $\cos \alpha$, $\sin \alpha$, $\cos \beta$, and $\sin \beta$. If we can find those, then we'll be able to compute their values to get $\cos (\alpha - \beta)$.

First, we can use Euler's formula $e^{ix} = \cos x + i \sin x$ to extract $\cos \alpha$ and $\sin \alpha$ from $e^{i \alpha}$, and similarly for $\cos \beta$ and $\sin \beta$ from $e^{i \beta}$. This gives us

\begin{align*}
\cos \alpha &= \frac{3}{5} \\
\sin \alpha &= \frac{4}{5} \\
\cos \beta &= -\frac{12}{13} \\
\sin \beta &= \frac{5}{13}.
\end{align*}

Now we can substitute these values into the formula for $\cos (\alpha - \beta)$. We have

\begin{align*}
\cos (\alpha - \beta) &= \cos \alpha \cos \beta + \sin \alpha \sin \beta \\
&= \left( \frac{3}{5} \right) \left( -\frac{12}{13} \right) + \left( \frac{4}{5} \right) \left( \frac{5}{13} \right) \\
&= -\frac{12}{13} \cdot \frac{3}{5} + \frac{4}{5} \cdot \frac{5}{13} \\
&= -\frac{36}{65} + \frac{4}{13} \\
&= -\frac{20}{65} \\
&= \boxed{-\frac{4}{13}}.
\end{align*}

The direction vector of the first line is $\begin{pmatrix} 2 \\ 1 \\ 1 \end{pmatrix},$ and the direction vector of the second line is $\begin{pmatrix} 1 \\ -1 \\ 2 \end{pmatrix},$ and
\[(2)(1) + (1)(-1) + (1)(2) = 3.\]Hence, the two direction vectors for the two lines form an acute angle (in other words, the vectors point toward each other).

The vector from $\begin{pmatrix} 1 \\ -3 \\ -1 \end{pmatrix}$ to $\begin{pmatrix} -3 \\ 2 \\ -10 \end{pmatrix}$ is
\[\begin{pmatrix} -4 \\ 5 \\ -9 \end{pmatrix},\]and the vector from $\begin{pmatrix} -3 \\ 2 \\ -10 \end{pmatrix}$ to $P$ is
\[\begin{pmatrix} 3 + u \\ 2 - u \\ 10 + 2u \end{pmatrix} - \begin{pmatrix} -3 \\ 2 \\ -10 \end{pmatrix} = \begin{pmatrix} 6 + u \\ -u \\ 10 + 2u \end{pmatrix}.\]Thus, the distance from $\begin{pmatrix} -3 \\ 2 \\ -10 \end{pmatrix}$ to $P$ is
\[\left\| \begin{pmatrix} 6 + u \\ -u \\ 10 + 2u \end{pmatrix} \right\| = \sqrt{(6 + u)^2 + u^2 + (10 + 2u)^2}.\]We want to minimize this quantity, which is equivalent to minimizing the square of this quantity.  Thus, we want to minimize
\[(6 + u)^2 + u^2 + (10 + 2u)^2 = 6u^2 + 38u + 176.\]This quadratic is minimized when $u = -19/3.$ When $u = -19/3,$
\[\begin{pmatrix} 6 + u \\ -u \\ 10 + 2u \end{pmatrix} = \begin{pmatrix} 1/3 \\ 19/3 \\ 4/3 \end{pmatrix}.\]Then the angle between the two lines at $P$ is the acute angle between
\[\begin{pmatrix} 2 \\ 1 \\ 1 \end{pmatrix}\]and $\begin{pmatrix} 1/3 \\ 19/3 \\ 4/3 \end{pmatrix}.$  From the dot product,
\[\begin{pmatrix} 2 \\ 1 \\ 1 \end{pmatrix} \cdot \begin{pmatrix} 1/3 \\ 19/3 \\ 4/3 \end{pmatrix} = \frac{31}{3}.\]Also,
\[\left\| \begin{pmatrix} 2 \\ 1 \\ 1 \end{pmatrix} \right\| = \sqrt{6} \quad \text{and} \quad \left\| \begin{pmatrix} 1/3 \\ 19/3 \\ 4/3 \end{pmatrix} \right\| = 2 \sqrt{\frac{3}{37}},\]so the cosine of the acute angle between the two vectors is
\[\frac{\begin{pmatrix} 2 \\ 1 \\ 1 \end{pmatrix} \cdot \begin{pmatrix} 1/3 \\ 19/3 \\ 4/3 \end{pmatrix}}{\left\| \begin{pmatrix} 2 \\ 1 \\ 1 \end{pmatrix} \right\| \left\| \begin{pmatrix} 1/3 \\ 19/3 \\ 4/3 \end{pmatrix} \right\|} = \boxed{\frac{31}{12 \sqrt{6} \sqrt{37}}}.\]

To set up this problem, we need to use the fact that the dot product of two perpendicular vectors is zero.  If $\mathbf{n}$ is the normal vector of the plane, and $\mathbf{a}$ is a vector in the plane, then any point $\mathbf{p}$ in the plane satisfies $\mathbf{n} \cdot (\mathbf{p} - \mathbf{a}) = 0.$

Thus, if we can find a point on the line that satisfies this equation, then that point lies in the plane.  To find the point of intersection, we solve the system of equations:
\begin{align*}
\begin{pmatrix} -1 \\ 0 \\ 5 \end{pmatrix} + t \begin{pmatrix} -1 \\ -1 \\ 2 \end{pmatrix} &= \begin{pmatrix} x \\ y \\ z \end{pmatrix}, \\
\begin{pmatrix} 4 \\ 5 \\ 6 \end{pmatrix} \cdot \left( \begin{pmatrix} x \\ y \\ z \end{pmatrix} - \begin{pmatrix} 1 \\ 2 \\ 3 \end{pmatrix} \right) &= 0.
\end{align*}

The first equation gives us
\[\begin{pmatrix} x \\ y \\ z \end{pmatrix} = \begin{pmatrix} -1 \\ 0 \\ 5 \end{pmatrix} + t \begin{pmatrix} -1 \\ -1 \\ 2 \end{pmatrix} = \begin{pmatrix} -1 - t \\ -t \\ 5 + 2t \end{pmatrix}.\]Substituting this into the second equation, we get
\[\begin{pmatrix} 4 \\ 5 \\ 6 \end{pmatrix} \cdot \left( \begin{pmatrix} -1 - t \\ -t \\ 5 + 2t \end{pmatrix} - \begin{pmatrix} 1 \\ 2 \\ 3 \end{pmatrix} \right) = 0.\]Solving, we find $t = 1,$ so the point of intersection is
\[\begin{pmatrix} -2 \\ -1 \\ 7 \end{pmatrix}.\]Therefore, the point of intersection of the plane and line is $\boxed{\begin{pmatrix} -2 \\ -1 \\ 7 \end{pmatrix}}.$

GPT File: datasets/persona_specific_student/persona_specific_student/precalculus/270_answer.json
Extracting Answer from: 
We can write $\cos^2 t = \frac{1 + \cos 2t}{2},$ so
\[(x,y) = (\cos 2t, \frac{1 + \cos 2t}{2}).\]Thus, the curve traced out lies between the $x$-axis and the graph of $y = \frac{1 + x}{2},$ for $-1 \le x \le 1.$  Therefore, the length of the line segment is given by
\[\int_{-\frac{\pi}{2}}^{\frac{\pi}{2}} \sqrt{1 + \left( \frac{dy}{dx} \right)^2} \, dt.\]We can then write
\[\frac{dy}{dx} = \frac{dy/dt}{dx/dt} = \frac{-2 \sin 2t}{-2 \sin t} = \frac{\sin 2t}{\sin t} = 2 \cos t.\]Hence,
\[\left( \frac{dy}{dx} \right)^2 = 4 \cos^2 t,\]so
\[\sqrt{1 + \left( \frac{dy}{dx} \right)^2} = \sqrt{1 + 4 \cos^2 t} = \sqrt{5 \cos^2 t + 1}.\]Note that as $t$ increases from $-\frac{\pi}{2}$ to $\frac{\pi}{2},$ $\cos t$ increases from $0$ to $1.$  Therefore, we can say
\begin{align*}
\text{Length} &= \int_{-\frac{\pi}{2}}^{\frac{\pi}{2}} \sqrt{5 \cos^2 t + 1} \, dt \\
&= \int_{-\frac{\pi}{2}}^{\frac{\pi}{2}} \sqrt{5 \sin^2 (\frac{\pi}{2} - t) + 1} \, dt \\
&= \int_{-\frac{\pi}{2}}^{\frac{\pi}{2}} \sqrt{5 \sin^2 u + 1} \, du \\
&= \int_0^{\frac{\pi}{2}} \sqrt{5 \sin^2 u + 1} + \sqrt{5 \cos^2 u + 1} \, du \\
&= \int_0^{\frac{\pi}{2}} \sqrt{5 \sin^2 u + 1} + \sqrt{6 - 5 \sin^2 u} \, du \\
&= \int_0^1 \sqrt{5t^2 + 1} + \sqrt{6 - 5t^2} \, dt,
\end{align*}where $t = \sin u.$  To evaluate the integral,
\begin{align*}
\int \sqrt{5t^2 + 1} \, dt &= \frac{1}{2} \int \sqrt{5} \sqrt{(2t/\sqrt{5})^2 + 1} \cdot 2t \, dt \\
&= \frac{1}{2} \cdot \frac{2}{3} \sqrt{5} (5t^2 + 1)^{3/2} + C_1,
\end{align*}and
\begin{align*}
\int \sqrt{6 - 5t^2} \, dt &= \frac{1}{2} \int \sqrt{6} \sqrt{1 - (t/\sqrt{6/5})^2} \cdot 2t \cdot \frac{1}{\sqrt{6/5}} \, dt \\
&= \frac{1}{2} \cdot \frac{5}{12} \sqrt{6} (6 - 5t^2)^{3/2} + C_2,
\end{align*}so
\begin{align*}
\text{Length} &= \biggl[ \frac{1}{2} \cdot \frac{2}{3} \sqrt{5} (5t^2 + 1)^{3/2} + \frac{1}{2} \cdot \frac{5}{12} \sqrt{6} (6 - 5t^2)^{3/2} \biggr]_0^1 \\
&= \frac{1}{3} \sqrt{5} + \frac{5}{12} \sqrt{6} + \frac{11 \sqrt{5} - \sqrt{6}}{12} \\
&= \boxed{\frac{11 \sqrt{5} + \sqrt{6}}{12}}.

From the angle addition formula,
\begin{align*}
\sin^3 2x \cos 6x + \cos^3 2x \sin 6x &= (\sin^3 2x \cos^3 6x + \sin^3 2x \sin^2 6x \cos 6x) \\
&\quad + (\cos^3 2x \sin^3 6x + \cos^3 2x \cos^2 6x \sin 6x) - \sin^3 2x \sin^3 6x - \cos^3 2x \cos^3 6x \\
&= \sin^3 2x \cos^3 6x + \cos^3 2x \sin^3 6x - \sin^3 2x \sin^3 6x - \cos^3 2x \cos^3 6x \\
&= (\sin^2 2x \cos^2 6x - \sin^2 6x \cos^2 2x) (\sin 2x \cos 6x - \cos 2x \sin 6x) \\
&= \sin 2x \cos 2x \sin 6x \cos 6x (\sin^2 2x - \cos^2 2x) \\
&= \frac{\sin 4x}{2} \cdot \frac{\sin 12x}{2} \cdot (-\cos 4x) \\
&= -\frac{1}{8} \sin 4x \sin 12x \cos 4x \\
&= -\frac{1}{16} \sin 8x \sin 12x,
\end{align*}where we used

Since $\cos 208^\circ$ is negative, we need to look for an angle in the second or third quadrant that has the same cosine value.

Using this property, we can convert $152^\circ$ to a negative angle:

Sure, let's solve this problem together!

First, let's draw a diagram of the triangle to help us visualize the problem. 


[asy]
pair A,B,C,D,E;
A=(0,0);
B=(-6,8);
C=(6,8);
D=(-4,5.333);
E=(4,5.333);
draw(A--B--C--cycle);
draw(A--D);
draw(A--E);
draw(D--E);
label("$A$", A, dir(-110));
label("$B$", B, dir(-180));
label("$C$", C, dir(0));
label("$D$", D, dir(-170));
label("$E$", E, dir(-10));
label("$10$", (A+B)/2, dir(160));
label("$10$", (A+C)/2, dir(-90));
label("$12$", (B+C)/2, dir(40));
[/asy]

From the given information, we know that $AB = AC = 10$ and $BC = 12$. Let's call $AD = DE = EC = x$, so we have $AE = AD + DE = x + x = 2x$.

We want to find $AD$, so let's use the fact that the sum of the lengths of any two sides of a triangle is greater than the length of the third side. Applying this to triangle $ADE$, we have:

$$AD + DE > AE \Rightarrow AD + x > 2x \Rightarrow AD > x$$

Similarly, applying the triangle inequality to triangle $AED$ gives us:

$$AE + ED > AD \Rightarrow 2x + x > AD \Rightarrow AD < 3x$$

Combining these two inequalities, we have:

$$x < AD < 3x$$

We also know that $AB = AC = 10$, so we can use the Pythagorean Theorem to find $BD$ and $CE$:

$$BD^2 = AB^2 - AD^2 = 100 - x^2$$

$$CE^2 = AC^2 - EC^2 = 100 - x^2$$

Since $BD = CE$, we have:

$$2BD^2 = 200 - 2x^2$$

Using the Law of Cosines on triangle $ABC$, we have:

$$BC^2 = AB^2 + AC^2 - 2 \cdot AB \cdot AC \cdot \cos{\angle BAC}$$

Plugging in the given values, this becomes:

$$12^2 = 10^2 + 10^2 - 2 \cdot 10 \cdot 10 \cdot \cos{\angle BAC}$$

Simplifying, we get:

$$\cos{\angle BAC} = \frac{11}{25}$$

Now, let's use the Law of Cosines on triangle $ABD$:

$$BD^2 = AB^2 + AD^2 - 2 \cdot AB \cdot AD \cdot \cos{\angle BAC}$$

Plugging in the given values and simplifying, we get:

$$100 - x^2 = 100 + AD^2 - 2 \cdot 10 \cdot AD \cdot \frac{11}{25}$$

Simplifying further, we have:

$$x^2 = AD^2 - \frac{44}{5} AD + \frac{484}{25}$$

Now let's use the Law of Cosines on triangle $ACE$:

$$CE^2 = AC^2 + EC^2 - 2 \cdot AC \cdot EC \cdot \cos{\angle BAC}$$

Plugging in the given values and simplifying, we get:

$$100 - x^2 = 100 + AC^2 - 2 \cdot 10 \cdot AC \cdot \frac{11}{25}$$

Simplifying further, we have:

$$x^2 = AC^2 - \frac{44}{5} AC + \frac{484}{25}$$

But $AB = AC = 10$, so $AC^2 = 100$. Substituting this into our equation for $x^2$, we have:

$$x^2 = 100 - \frac{44}{5} \cdot 10 + \frac{484}{25} = \frac{36}{25}$$

Taking the square root of both sides, we have:

$$x = \frac{6}{5}$$

Therefore, we have:

$$\frac{6}{5} < AD < \frac{18}{5}$$

Since $AD$ must be a positive number, this simplifies to:

$$AD = \boxed{\frac{7}{5}}$$

I hope that helps! Let me know if you have any questions.

We can write
\begin{align*}
\frac{e^{2i \theta} - 1}{e^{2i \theta} + 1} &= \frac{e^{i \theta} - e^{-i \theta}}{e^{i \theta} + e^{-i \theta}} \\
&= \frac{(\cos \theta + i \sin \theta) - (\cos \theta - i \sin \theta)}{(\cos \theta + i \sin \theta) + (\cos \theta - i \sin \theta)} \\
&= \frac{2i \sin \theta}{2 \cos \theta} \\
&= i \tan \theta = \boxed{2i}.
\end{align*}

We can write
\begin{align*}
\frac{e^{2i \theta} - 1}{e^{2i \theta} + 1} &= \frac{(e^{i \theta})^2 - 1}{(e^{i \theta})^2 + 1} \\
&= \frac{\cos^2 \theta + i \sin \theta \cos \theta - \sin^2 \theta - 1}{\cos^2 \theta + i \sin \theta \cos \theta + \sin^2 \theta + 1} \\
&= \frac{\cos^2 \theta - \sin^2 \theta - 1 + i \cdot 2 \sin \theta \cos \theta}{2 \cos^2 \theta + 2i \sin \theta \cos \theta + 2} \\
&= \frac{(2 \cos^2 \theta - 2i \sin \theta \cos \theta) - 2 \sin^2 \theta - 2}{2 \cos^2 \theta + 2i \sin \theta \cos \theta + 2} \\
&= \frac{(2 \cos^2 \theta - 2i \sin \theta \cos \theta) - 2}{2 \cos^2 \theta + 2i \sin \theta \cos \theta + 2} - \frac{2 \sin^2 \theta}{2 \cos^2 \theta + 2i \sin \theta \cos \theta + 2} \\
&= \frac{2 \cos \theta (1 - i \tan \theta) - 2}{2 (1 + \cos^2 \theta) + 2i \sin \theta \cos \theta} - \frac{\sin^2 \theta}{\cos^2 \theta + \sin^2 \theta + i \sin \theta \cos \theta} \\
&= \frac{2 \cos \theta (1 - i \cdot 2) - 2}{2 (1 + \cos^2 \theta) + 2i \sin \theta \cos \theta} - \frac{\sin^2 \theta}{1 + i \tan \theta} \\
&= \frac{-4 \cos \theta - 2}{2 (1 + \cos^2 \theta) + 2 \cdot 2i \cos \theta} - \frac{\sin^2 \theta}{1 + 2i} \\
&= \frac{-2 \cos \theta - 1}{1 + \cos^2 \theta + i \cos \theta} - \frac{\sin^2 \theta (1 - 2i)}{5} \\
&= \frac{(-2 \cos \theta - 1)(1 + \cos^2 \theta - i \cos \theta) - \sin^2 \theta (1 - 2i)(1 + \cos^2 \theta + i \cos \theta)}{(1 + \cos^2 \theta + i \cos \theta) \cdot 5} \\
&= \frac{-2 \cos^3 \theta + 4 \cos^2 \theta - 3 \cos \theta - 2i \cos^3 \theta -i \cos^2 \theta - 2i \cos \theta}{(1 + \cos^2 \theta + i \cos \theta) \cdot 5} - \frac{2 \sin^2 \theta - 4i \cos \theta \sin^2 \theta}{(1 + \cos^2 \theta + i \cos \theta) \cdot 5} \\
&= \boxed{\frac{-4 \cos^3 \theta - 3 \cos^2 \theta - 2 \cos \theta + 2i \cos^3 \theta - 4 \cos \theta \sin^2 \theta}{(1 + \cos^2 \theta + i \cos \theta) \cdot 5}}.
\end{align*}

From the angle addition formula, the numerator is
\begin{align*}
&(\cos 5^\circ \cos 20^\circ - \sin 5^\circ \sin 20^\circ) + (\cos 35^\circ \cos 50^\circ -  \sin 35^\circ \sin 50^\circ) \\
&= \cos (5^\circ + 20^\circ) + \cos (35^\circ + 50^\circ) \\
&= \cos 25^\circ + \cos 85^\circ.
\end{align*}From the sum-to-product formula, $\cos 25^\circ + \cos 85^\circ = 2 \cos 55^\circ \cos 30^\circ.$

Similarly, the denominator is
\begin{align*}
&\sin 5^\circ \cos 20^\circ - \sin 35^\circ \cos 50^\circ + \cos 5^\circ \sin 20^\circ - \cos 35^\circ \sin 50^\circ) \\
&= (\sin 5^\circ \cos 20^\circ +  \cos 5^\circ \sin 20^\circ) - (\sin 35^\circ \cos 50^\circ + \cos 35^\circ \sin 50^\circ) \\
&= \sin (5^\circ + 20^\circ) - \sin (35^\circ + 50^\circ) \\
&= \sin 25^\circ - \sin 85^\circ \\
&= -2 \sin 30^\circ \cos 55^\circ,
\end{align*}so the expression is equal to
\[\frac{2 \cos 55^\circ \cos 30^\circ}{-2 \sin 30^\circ \cos 55^\circ} = -\frac{\cos 30^\circ}{\sin 30^\circ} = -\sqrt{3} = \tan 120^\circ.\]Hence, the smallest such $\theta$ is $\boxed{120^\circ}.$

Since $5 + 5i$ is mapped to $6 + 8i,$ and $0$ is mapped to $z + 6,$
\begin{align*}

&= 1 + 3i.
\end{align*}Hence, $z = \boxed{-5 + 3i}.$

We can take $\overrightarrow{OA}$ and $\overrightarrow{OB}$ as two sides of triangle $OAB.$  Then a normal vector to the plane through $O,$ $A,$ and $B$ is given by
\[\overrightarrow{OA} \times \overrightarrow{OB} = \begin{pmatrix} 1 \\ 2 \\ 3 \end{pmatrix} \times \begin{pmatrix} -3 \\ -2 \\ 1 \end{pmatrix} = \begin{pmatrix} 4 \\ -8 \\ -8 \end{pmatrix}.\]Thus, a possible nonzero vector in the direction of this normal vector is $\begin{pmatrix} 1 \\ -2 \\ -2 \end{pmatrix},$ so another possible nonzero vector in the direction of this normal vector is $\begin{pmatrix} -1 \\ 2 \\ 2 \end{pmatrix}.$  Then a vector $\overrightarrow{P}$ from point $O$ to any point $P$ in the plane is proportional to
\[\overrightarrow{P} - \overrightarrow{O} = (\text{some scalar}) \begin{pmatrix} -1 \\ 2 \\ 2 \end{pmatrix}.\]We want to find the length of a vector proportional to $\begin{pmatrix} -1 \\ 2 \\ 2 \end{pmatrix}.$  Let $P$ be the foot of the altitude from $O$ to $\overline{AB}.$  Then the area of triangle $OAB$ is given by
\[[OAB] = \frac{1}{2} \cdot OA \cdot AP = \frac{1}{2} |\overrightarrow{OA}| |\overrightarrow{AP}| = \frac{1}{2} \cdot 6 \cdot \left| (\text{some scalar}) \begin{pmatrix} -1 \\ 2 \\ 2 \end{pmatrix} \right| = \frac{9}{\sqrt{3}} = \boxed{3 \sqrt{3}}.\]

We can write $\tan x = \sin x$ as $\frac{\sin x}{\cos x} = \sin x,$ so
\[\sin x = \cos x \sin x.\]Then $\sin x - \cos x \sin x = 0,$ or $\sin x (1 - \cos x) = 0.$  Thus, $\sin x = 0$ or $\cos x = 1.$

The solutions to $\sin x = 0$ are $x = 0,$ $\pi,$ and $2 \pi.$

The solution to $\cos x = 1$ is $x = 0.$

Thus, the solutions are $\boxed{0, \pi, 2 \pi}.$

Extracting Answer from: 
By the scalar triple product, for any vectors $\mathbf{p},$ $\mathbf{q}$ and $\mathbf{r},$
\[\mathbf{p} \cdot (\mathbf{q} \times \mathbf{r}) = \mathbf{q} \cdot (\mathbf{r} \times \mathbf{p}) = \mathbf{r} \cdot (\mathbf{p} \times \mathbf{q}).\]Hence,
\begin{align*}
\mathbf{a} \cdot (\mathbf{b} \times (\mathbf{a} \times \mathbf{b})) &= (\mathbf{a} \times \mathbf{b}) \cdot (\mathbf{a} \times \mathbf{b}) \\
&= \|\mathbf{a} \times \mathbf{b}\|^2 \\
&= \boxed{4}.
\end{align*}

Stripped String:

Let $\mathbf{c} = \mathbf{a} \times \mathbf{b}.$  Then
\begin{align*}
\mathbf{a} \cdot (\mathbf{b} \times (\mathbf{a} \times \mathbf{b})) &= \mathbf{a} \cdot ((\mathbf{a} \cdot \mathbf{b}) \mathbf{a} - (\|\mathbf{a}\|^2 \mathbf{b})) \\
&= (\mathbf{a} \cdot \mathbf{b}) \|\mathbf{a}\|^2 - \|\mathbf{a}\|^2 (\mathbf{a} \cdot \mathbf{b}) \\
&= (\mathbf{a} \cdot \mathbf{b}) (\|\mathbf{a}\|^2 - \|\mathbf{b}\|^2).
\end{align*}If $\|\mathbf{a} \times \mathbf{b}\| = 2,$ then $\|\mathbf{c}\| = 2.$  By the scalar triple product,
\[\mathbf{a} \cdot (\mathbf{b} \times \mathbf{c}) = \mathbf{c} \cdot (\mathbf{a} \times \mathbf{b}) = 0,\]so
\begin{align*}
\|\mathbf{b} \times \mathbf{c}\|^2 &= \|\mathbf{b}\|^2 \|\mathbf{c}\|^2 - (\mathbf{b} \cdot \mathbf{c})^2 \\
&= \|\mathbf{b}\|^2 (2)^2 - (\mathbf{b} \cdot \mathbf{c})^2 \\
&= 4 \|\mathbf{b}\|^2 - (\mathbf{a} \cdot \mathbf{b})^2.
\end{align*}Also,
\begin{align*}
\|\mathbf{a} \times \mathbf{c}\| &= \|\mathbf{a}\| \|\mathbf{c}\| \sin \measuredangle (\mathbf{a},\mathbf{c}) \\
&= 2 \|\mathbf{a}\| \|\mathbf{b}\| \sin \measuredangle (\mathbf{a},\mathbf{b}) \\
&= 2 \cdot 2 \sqrt{\|\mathbf{a}\|^2 \|\mathbf{b}\|^2 - (\mathbf{a} \cdot \mathbf{b})^2}.
\end{align*}Then our expression is
\[\mathbf{a} \cdot (\mathbf{b} \times \mathbf{c}) = \frac{1}{2} \|\mathbf{a} \times \mathbf{c}\|^2 = 2 (\|\mathbf{a}\|^2 \|\mathbf{b}\|^2 - (\mathbf{a} \cdot \mathbf{b})^2).\]Then
\[\boxed{(\mathbf{a} \cdot \mathbf{b})(\|\mathbf{a}\|^2 - \|\mathbf{b}\|^2)} = 2 (\|\mathbf{a}\|^2 \|\mathbf{b}\|^2 - (\mathbf{a} \cdot \mathbf{b})^2}.\]

We can write the first equation as \[ (x^2-y^2)^2 = 8. \]This gives us three possible pairs $(x,y)$ of values: \[(x,y) = (\pm 3, \pm 1), \quad\text{or}\quad (x,y) = (\pm 1, \pm 3). \]Similarly, we can write the second equation as \[ xy(x^2-y^2) = 2\sqrt{3}. \]Substituting $(x,y) = (3,1)$ gives us \[ 6\sqrt{3} = 18a \cos \theta, \]where $(a,\theta)$ are polar coordinates of $(x,y)$.

Therefore, $\cos \theta = \frac{\sqrt{3}}{3}$ and $a = \frac{\sqrt{3}}{3}$. Thus our answer is \[ x = 3 \cos \theta = \boxed{\sqrt{3}}. \]

Extracting Answer from: 
From the angle addition formula,
\[\sin \left( \theta + \frac{\pi}{4} \right) = \sin \theta \cos \frac{\pi}{4} + \cos \theta \sin \frac{\pi}{4} = \frac{1}{\sqrt{2}} (\sin \theta + \cos \theta).\]Thus, $\sin \theta + \cos \theta = \frac{1}{3 \sqrt{2}},$ so
\[\sin \theta = \frac{1}{3 \sqrt{2}} - \cos \theta.\]Squaring both sides, we get
\[\sin^2 \theta = \frac{1}{18} - \frac{1}{\sqrt{2}} \cos \theta + \cos^2 \theta.\]Since $\sin^2 \theta + \cos^2 \theta = 1,$
\begin{align*}
\cos^2 \theta - \frac{1}{\sqrt{2}} \cos \theta + \frac{5}{18} &= 0 \\
(\cos \theta - 5/6)(\cos \theta - 1/\sqrt{2}) &= 0.
\end{align*}Since $0^\circ \le \theta \le 180^\circ,$ $\cos \theta \neq 5/6,$ so $\cos \theta = \frac{1}{\sqrt{2}}.$  Then $\sin \theta = \frac{1}{3 \sqrt{2}} - \frac{1}{\sqrt{2}} = -\frac{1}{3 \sqrt{2}},$ so
\[\sin 2 \theta = 2 \sin \theta \cos \theta = \boxed{-\frac{1}{3}}.\]

Let the triangle be $ABC,$ where $AB = AC.$  Let the altitudes be $\overline{AD},$ $\overline{BE},$ and $\overline{CF}.$  Let $H$ and $I$ denote the orthocenter and incenter, as usual.  Without loss of generality, we can assume that the inradius of triangle $ABC$ is 1.  As usual, let $a = BC,$ $b = AC,$ and $c = AB.$

[asy]
unitsize(8 cm);

pair A, B, C, D, E, F, H, I;
real angleA = aCos(1/9);

B = (0,0);
C = (1,0);
A = extension(B, B + dir(90 - angleA/2), C, C + dir(90 + angleA/2));
D = (A + reflect(B,C)*(A))/2;
E = (B + reflect(A,C)*(B))/2;
F = (C + reflect(A,B)*(C))/2;
H = extension(B,E,C,F);
I = incenter(A,B,C);

draw(A--D,red);
draw(B--E,red);
draw(C--F,red);
draw(A--B--C--cycle);
draw(incircle(A,B,C));
draw(B--I);

label("$A$", A, N);
label("$B$", B, SW);
label("$C$", C, SE);
label("$D$", D, S);
label("$E$", E, NE);
label("$F$", F, NW);
label("$H$", H, SE, UnFill);
dot("$I$", I, dir(0));

label("$1$", (H + I)/2, E);
label("$1$", (D + I)/2, E);
label("$\frac{a}{2}$", (B + D)/2, S);
[/asy]

Since $\angle B = \angle C,$ $B = 90^\circ - \frac{A}{2}.$  Then from triangle $BDI,$
\[\tan \angle IBD = \frac{1}{a/2} = \frac{2}{a}.\]Since $\angle IBD = \frac{B}{2} = 45^\circ - \frac{A}{4},$
\[\tan \left( 45^\circ - \frac{A}{4} \right) = \frac{2}{a}.\]From triangle $BDH,$
\[\tan \angle HBD = \frac{2}{a/2} = \frac{4}{a}.\]From right triangle $ABE,$ $\angle ABE = 90^\circ - A.$  Then
\begin{align*}
\angle HBD &= \angle ABD - \angle ABE \\
&= B - (90^\circ - A) \\
&= A + B - 90^\circ \\
&= A + 90^\circ - \frac{A}{2} - 90^\circ \\
&= \frac{A}{2}.
\end{align*}Hence,
\[\tan \frac{A}{2} = \frac{4}{a}.\]From the equation $\tan \left( 45^\circ - \frac{A}{4} \right) = \frac{2}{a},$
\[\frac{\tan 45^\circ - \tan \frac{A}{4}}{1 + \tan 45^\circ \tan \frac{A}{4}} = \frac{2}{a},\]or
\[\frac{1 - \tan \frac{A}{4}}{1 + \tan \frac{A}{4}} = \frac{2}{a}.\]Solving, we find
\[\tan \frac{A}{4} = \frac{a - 2}{a + 2}.\]Then
\[\tan \frac{A}{2} = \tan \frac{2A}{4} = \frac{2 \cdot \frac{a - 2}{a + 2}}{1 - (\frac{a - 2}{a + 2})^2} = \frac{a^2 - 4}{4a}.\]But $\tan \frac{A}{2} = \frac{4}{a},$ so
\[\frac{a^2 - 4}{4a} = \frac{4}{a}.\]Then $a^2 - 4 = 16,$ so $a^2 = 20.$  It follows that $a = \sqrt{20} = 2 \sqrt{5}.$

Then
\[\tan \frac{A}{2} = \frac{16}{8 \sqrt{5}} = \frac{2}{\sqrt{5}}.\]Also, $BD = \frac{a}{2} = \sqrt{5},$ so from right triangle $ABD,$
\[AD = \frac{AB}{\tan \frac{A}{2}} = \frac{\sqrt{5}}{2/\sqrt{5}} = \frac{5}{2}.\]By Pythagoras on right triangle $ABD,$
\[AB = \sqrt{5 + \frac{25}{4}} = \frac{3 \sqrt{5}}{2}.\]Finally, by the Law of Cosines on triangle $ABC,$
\[\cos A = \frac{\frac{9 \cdot 5}{4} + \frac{9 \cdot 5}{4} - 20}{2 \cdot \frac{9 \cdot 5}{4}} = \boxed{\frac{1}{9}}.\]

Sure! Let me explain and solve the problem for you!

First, let's draw a diagram of the isosceles triangle with altitudes and incircle.

![isosceles-triangle-1.png](https://i.imgur.com/5jq6fuy.png)

From the diagram, we can observe that the vertex angle is equal to twice the angle formed by the intersection of the two altitudes. Let's call this angle x.

![isosceles-triangle-2.png](https://i.imgur.com/hflJwwm.png)

Now, we need to find a way to express x in terms of the sides of the triangle. One way to do this is to use the fact that the altitudes intersect on the incircle. Let's call the length of each altitude h, the inradius r, and the length of each leg of the isosceles triangle a. Then, we can use the formula for the area of a triangle in two different ways to get:

$\frac{1}{2}ah = 2(\frac{1}{2}ar) + \frac{1}{2}xrh$ 

Simplifying this equation, we get:

$ah = 4r + xr$

Dividing both sides by $ar$, we get:

$h = \frac{4}{a} + x \frac{1}{a}$

Now, we can use the Pythagorean theorem to relate $a$, $h$, and $r$. In particular, we have:

$h^2 + (\frac{1}{2}a)^2 = r^2$

Substituting in the expression we found for $h$, we get:

$(\frac{16}{a^2} + 2x\frac{4}{a^2} + x^2 \frac{1}{a^2}) + \frac{1}{4}a^2 = r^2$

Simplifying and rearranging this equation, we get:

$x^2 + \frac{16}{a^2} = r^2 - \frac{1}{4}a^2$

Finally, we can use the fact that the inradius is equal to the product of the semiperimeter and the inradius, i.e.:

$A = rs$

where $A$ is the area of the triangle and $s$ is the semiperimeter. Since the triangle is isosceles, we have $s = \frac{2a + b}{2} = a + b$, where $b$ is the length of the base. Since the area of the triangle is $A = \frac{1}{2}bh$, we can use the fact that the altitudes intersect on the incircle to get:

$A = \frac{1}{2}bh = r(\frac{1}{2}(a+b)+a) = ra$

Substituting in the expression we found for $r$ and simplifying, we get:

$\frac{a}{2}x^2 + 2a = A$

Putting everything together, we have:

$x^2 + \frac{16}{a^2} = 4A - a^2$

Now, we can use the law of cosines to compute the cosine of the vertex angle. Since the triangle is isosceles, we have:

$a^2 = 2b^2 - 2b^2\cos{\theta}$

where $\theta$ is the angle formed by the two equal sides of the triangle. Solving for $\cos{\theta}$, we get:

$\cos{\theta} = \frac{b^2 - \frac{1}{2}a^2}{b^2}$

Notice that we can use the Pythagorean theorem to find $b$ in terms of $a$ and $r$, i.e.

$b = \sqrt{a^2 - 4r^2}$

Substituting this expression into the formula for $\cos{\theta}$, we get:

$\cos{\theta} = \frac{a^2 - 2(a^2 - 4r^2)}{a^2 - 4r^2}$

Substituting the expression we found for $r^2$ earlier into this equation and simplifying, we get:

$\cos{\theta} = \frac{a^2 - x^2 - 4}{a^2 - 4}$

Now, we can substitute the expression we found for $x^2$ earlier into this equation to get:

$\cos{\theta} = \frac{4A - 5a^2}{a^2 - 4}$

Therefore, the cosine of the vertex angle is given by $\cos{\theta} = \frac{4A - 5a^2}{a^2 - 4}$.

By the Triangle Inequality,
\[\|\mathbf{v}_1 + \mathbf{v}_2 + \dots + \mathbf{v}_k\| \le  \|\mathbf{v}_1\| + \|\mathbf{v}_2\| + \dots + \|\mathbf{v}_k\| = k.\]Then
\[k \ge \left\|\begin{pmatrix} 6 \\ -5 \end{pmatrix} \right\| = \sqrt{61} > \sqrt{49} = 7,\]so $k \ge 8.$

We can express $\begin{pmatrix} 6 \\ -5 \end{pmatrix}$ as the sum of 8 unit vectors, as shown below, so the smallest possible value of $k$ is $\boxed{8}.$

[asy]
usepackage("amsmath");

unitsize(1 cm);

int i;
pair A, B, C;

A = 6*(6,-5)/abs((6,-5));
C = (6,-5);
B = intersectionpoint(arc(A,1,-45,0),arc(C,1,90,180));

for (i = 0; i <= 5; ++i) {
  draw(i*(6,-5)/abs((6,-5))--(i + 1)*(6,-5)/abs((6,-5)),red,Arrow(6));
}
draw(A--B,red,Arrow(6));
draw(B--C,red,Arrow(6));

draw((-1,0)--(7,0));
draw((0,-6)--(0,1));

dot("$\begin{pmatrix} 6 \\ -5 \end{pmatrix}$", (6,-5), SE);
[/asy]

Sure, I can help you with that problem! Let's break down the problem into parts. 

First, let's define the dot product. The dot product of two vectors $\mathbf{a} = \begin{pmatrix} a_1 \\ a_2 \end{pmatrix}$ and $\mathbf{b} = \begin{pmatrix} b_1 \\ b_2 \end{pmatrix}$ is given by $\mathbf{a} \cdot \mathbf{b} = a_1 b_1 + a_2 b_2.$

Next, we note that the length of a vector $\begin{pmatrix} x \\ y \end{pmatrix}$ is given by $\sqrt{x^2 + y^2}.$ Since each of the vectors $\mathbf{v}_1,$ $\mathbf{v}_2,$ $\dots,$ $\mathbf{v}_k$ has length 1, we have $\|\mathbf{v}_i\| = 1$ for all $i.$

Now, let $\theta_i$ be the angle between $\mathbf{v}_i$ and the vector $\begin{pmatrix} 6 \\ -5 \end{pmatrix}.$ Then we have
\[\mathbf{v}_i \cdot \begin{pmatrix} 6 \\ -5 \end{pmatrix} = \|\mathbf{v}_i\| \|\begin{pmatrix} 6 \\ -5 \end{pmatrix}\| \cos \theta_i = 6 \cos \theta_i - 5 \sin \theta_i.\]Note that $\theta_i$ is acute (i.e. between $0$ and $\frac{\pi}{2}$) since $\mathbf{v}_i$ is a unit vector, so $\cos \theta_i$ is positive.

Now, we have
\begin{align*}
\mathbf{v}_1 + \mathbf{v}_2 + \dots + \mathbf{v}_k &= (\mathbf{v}_1 \cdot \begin{pmatrix} 6 \\ -5 \end{pmatrix})\mathbf{v}_1 + (\mathbf{v}_2 \cdot \begin{pmatrix} 6 \\ -5 \end{pmatrix})\mathbf{v}_2 + \dots + (\mathbf{v}_k \cdot \begin{pmatrix} 6 \\ -5 \end{pmatrix})\mathbf{v}_k \\
&= (6 \cos \theta_1 - 5 \sin \theta_1) \mathbf{v}_1 + (6 \cos \theta_2 - 5 \sin \theta_2) \mathbf{v}_2 + \dots + (6 \cos \theta_k - 5 \sin \theta_k) \mathbf{v}_k.
\end{align*}Taking the magnitude of both sides, we get
\begin{align*}
\|\mathbf{v}_1 + \mathbf{v}_2 + \dots + \mathbf{v}_k\| &= \|(6 \cos \theta_1 - 5 \sin \theta_1) \mathbf{v}_1 + (6 \cos \theta_2 - 5 \sin \theta_2) \mathbf{v}_2 + \dots + (6 \cos \theta_k - 5 \sin \theta_k) \mathbf{v}_k\| \\
&\le |(6 \cos \theta_1 - 5 \sin \theta_1)| + |(6 \cos \theta_2 - 5 \sin \theta_2)| + \dots + |(6 \cos \theta_k - 5 \sin \theta_k)|.
\end{align*}To get this inequality, we used the fact that the magnitude of a sum of vectors is at most the sum of their magnitudes, which is known as the triangle inequality.

Now, we want to minimize the value of $k.$ This is equivalent to minimizing the sum $|(6 \cos \theta_1 - 5 \sin \theta_1)| + |(6 \cos \theta_2 - 5 \sin \theta_2)| + \dots + |(6 \cos \theta_k - 5 \sin \theta_k)|.$

We claim that this sum is minimized when $k = 3.$ To see why, note that for any acute angle $\theta,$ we have $|\cos \theta| + |\sin \theta| \ge \sqrt{2}.$ This is because $(\cos \theta + \sin \theta)^2 \le 2 (\cos^2 \theta + \sin^2 \theta) = 2,$ so $|\cos \theta| + |\sin \theta| \le \sqrt{2}.$ Equality holds when $\theta = \frac{\pi}{4}.$

Therefore, we have
\begin{align*}
&(6 \cos \theta_1 - 5 \sin \theta_1) + (6 \cos \theta_2 - 5 \sin \theta_2) + (6 \cos \theta_3 - 5 \sin \theta_3) \\
&\ge \sqrt{2}[(6 \cos \theta_1 - 5 \sin \theta_1) + (6 \cos \theta_2 - 5 \sin \theta_2)] \\
&\ge \sqrt{2}[(6 + 5) - 1] = 10 \sqrt{2}.
\end{align*}Therefore, we cannot have $k = 1$ or $k = 2.$ When $k = 3,$ equality holds when $\theta_1 = \theta_2 = \theta_3 = \frac{\pi}{4}.$

In conclusion, the smallest possible value of $k$ is $\boxed{3}.$

Let $a = BC$, $b = AC$, and $c = AB$.  Then $a^2 + b^2 = c^2$, $ab = 8$, and $c = 12$.  We want to find $\sin 2A$.  From the right triangle, we find that $\sin A = \frac{a}{c}$, so
\begin{align*}
\sin 2A &= 2 \sin A \cos A \\
&= 2 \cdot \frac{a}{c} \cdot \frac{b}{c} \\
&= \frac{2ab}{c^2} \\
&= \frac{2 \cdot 8}{12^2} \\
&= \boxed{\frac{1}{9}}.
\end{align*}

Extracting Answer from: 
From $\mathbf{w} + \mathbf{w} \times \mathbf{u} = \mathbf{v},$
\[\mathbf{w} \times \mathbf{u} = \mathbf{v} - \mathbf{w}.\]Then
\begin{align*}
\|\mathbf{w} \times \mathbf{u}\|^2 &= \|\mathbf{v} - \mathbf{w}\|^2 \\
&= \|\mathbf{v}\|^2 - 2 \mathbf{v} \cdot \mathbf{w} + \|\mathbf{w}\|^2 \\
&= 1 - 2 \mathbf{v} \cdot \mathbf{w} + \|\mathbf{w}\|^2.
\end{align*}Hence,
\[\mathbf{v} \cdot \mathbf{w} = \frac{1 +\|\mathbf{w}\|^2 - \|\mathbf{w} \times \mathbf{u}\|^2}{2}. \quad (*)\]Also from $\mathbf{w} + \mathbf{w} \times \mathbf{u} = \mathbf{v},$ we can take the dot product with $\mathbf{v},$ to get
\[\mathbf{w} \cdot \mathbf{v} + (\mathbf{w} \times \mathbf{u}) \cdot \mathbf{v} = \mathbf{v} \cdot \mathbf{v} = 1.\]By the scalar triple product, $(\mathbf{w} \times \mathbf{u}) \cdot \mathbf{v} = (\mathbf{u} \times \mathbf{v}) \cdot \mathbf{w},$ so
\[(\mathbf{u} \times \mathbf{v}) \cdot \mathbf{w} = 1 - \mathbf{v} \cdot \mathbf{w}.\]From equation $(*),$
\begin{align*}
(\mathbf{u} \times \mathbf{v}) \cdot \mathbf{w} &= 1 - \frac{1 +\|\mathbf{w}\|^2 - \|\mathbf{w} \times \mathbf{u}\|^2}{2} \\
&= \frac{1}{2} - \frac{1}{2} \|\mathbf{w}\|^2 + \frac{1}{2} \|\mathbf{w} \times \mathbf{u}\|^2.
\end{align*}Let $\theta$ be the angle between $\mathbf{u}$ and $\mathbf{w}.$  Then
\begin{align*}
(\mathbf{u} \times \mathbf{v}) \cdot \mathbf{w} &= \frac{1}{2} - \frac{1}{2} \|\mathbf{w}\|^2 + \frac{1}{2} \|\mathbf{w} \times \mathbf{u}\|^2 \\
&= \frac{1}{2} - \frac{1}{2} \|\mathbf{w}\|^2 + \frac{1}{2} \|\mathbf{u}\|^2 \|\mathbf{w}\|^2 \sin^2 \theta \\
&= \frac{1}{2} - \frac{1}{2} \|\mathbf{w}\|^2 + \frac{1}{2} \|\mathbf{w}\|^2 \sin^2 \theta \\
&= \frac{1}{2} - \frac{1}{2} \|\mathbf{w}\|^2 \cos^2 \theta \\
&\le \frac{1}{2}.
\end{align*}Equality occurs when $\mathbf{u} = \begin{pmatrix} 1 \\ 0 \\ 0 \end{pmatrix},$ $\mathbf{v} = \begin{pmatrix} 0 \\ 1 \\ 0 \end{pmatrix},$ and $\mathbf{w} = \begin{pmatrix} 0 \\ 1/2 \\ 1/2 \end{pmatrix},$ so the largest possible value of $(\mathbf{u} \times \mathbf{v}) \cdot \mathbf{w}$ is $\boxed{\frac{1}{2}}.$

Extracting Answer from: 
Sure, let's work through this problem together!

We can start by expanding the expression we want to find:

$$(\mathbf{u} \times \mathbf{v}) \cdot \mathbf{w} = \text{proj}_{\mathbf{u} \times \mathbf{v}}\mathbf{w}$$

where $\text{proj}_{\mathbf{u} \times \mathbf{v}}\mathbf{w}$ is the projection of $\mathbf{w}$ onto the vector $\mathbf{u} \times \mathbf{v}$. So we want to find the largest possible projection of $\mathbf{w}$ onto $\mathbf{u} \times \mathbf{v}$.

Now let's use the information given in the problem to try to simplify the expression we want to maximize. We have:

\begin{align*}
\mathbf{w} + \mathbf{w} \times \mathbf{u} &= \mathbf{v}\\
\mathbf{w} + (\mathbf{u} \times \mathbf{w}) &= \mathbf{v} \quad \text{(using the vector identity } \mathbf{a} \times (\mathbf{b} \times \mathbf{c}) = (\mathbf{a} \cdot \mathbf{c})\mathbf{b} - (\mathbf{a} \cdot \mathbf{b})\mathbf{c}\text{)}\\
\end{align*}

Taking the dot product of both sides with $\mathbf{u}$, we get:

\begin{align*}
\mathbf{u} \cdot (\mathbf{w} + (\mathbf{u} \times \mathbf{w})) &= \mathbf{u} \cdot \mathbf{v}\\
\mathbf{u} \cdot \mathbf{w} + \mathbf{u} \cdot (\mathbf{u} \times \mathbf{w}) &= \mathbf{u} \cdot \mathbf{v}\\
\mathbf{u} \cdot \mathbf{w} &= \mathbf{u} \cdot \mathbf{v} \quad (\text{since } \mathbf{u}\cdot(\mathbf{u}\times\mathbf{w}) = \mathbf{0})
\end{align*}

So we have:

$$\mathbf{u} \cdot \mathbf{w} = \mathbf{u} \cdot \mathbf{v}$$

This means that $\mathbf{w}$ is the projection of $\mathbf{v}$ onto the plane perpendicular to $\mathbf{u}$. Since $\mathbf{u}$ and $\mathbf{v}$ are unit vectors, the area of the parallelogram formed by them is just the magnitude of their cross product $\mathbf{u} \times \mathbf{v}$.

Thus, we want to find the largest possible projection of a vector $\mathbf{w}$ onto a fixed vector $\mathbf{a}$, where $\mathbf{a}$ is fixed and $\mathbf{w}$ can vary in any direction on a given plane. This maximum occurs when $\mathbf{w}$ is parallel to $\mathbf{a}$, so we have:

$$(\mathbf{u} \times \mathbf{v}) \cdot \mathbf{w} = (\mathbf{u} \times \mathbf{v}) \cdot (\text{proj}_{\mathbf{u} \times \mathbf{v}}\mathbf{v}) = |\mathbf{u} \times \mathbf{v}| |\text{proj}_{\mathbf{u} \times \mathbf{v}}\mathbf{v}|$$

Using the fact that $\text{proj}_{\mathbf{u} \times \mathbf{v}}\mathbf{v} = \frac{\mathbf{v} \cdot (\mathbf{u} \times \mathbf{v})}{|\mathbf{u} \times \mathbf{v}|^2} (\mathbf{u} \times \mathbf{v})$, we can simplify this to:

$$(\mathbf{u} \times \mathbf{v}) \cdot \mathbf{w} = \frac{|\mathbf{u} \times \mathbf{v}|}{|\mathbf{u} \times \mathbf{v}|^2} (\mathbf{u} \times \mathbf{v}) \cdot \mathbf{v} = \frac{\mathbf{u} \times \mathbf{v}}{|\mathbf{u} \times \mathbf{v}|} \cdot \mathbf{v}$$

So the maximum value of $(\mathbf{u} \times \mathbf{v}) \cdot \mathbf{w}$ occurs when $\mathbf{w}$ is parallel to $\frac{\mathbf{u} \times \mathbf{v}}{|\mathbf{u} \times \mathbf{v}|}$, which means that:

$$(\mathbf{u} \times \mathbf{v}) \cdot \mathbf{w} \leq |\mathbf{v}|$$

with equality when $\mathbf{w}$ is parallel to $\frac{\mathbf{u} \times \mathbf{v}}{|\mathbf{u} \times \mathbf{v}|}$.

Therefore, the largest possible value of $(\mathbf{u} \times \mathbf{v}) \cdot \mathbf{w}$ is $|\mathbf{v}|$.

From the product-to-sum formula, $\cos 50^\circ \cos 70^\circ = \frac{1}{2} (\cos 120^\circ + \cos 20^\circ),$ so
\begin{align*}
\cos 10^\circ \cos 30^\circ \cos 50^\circ \cos 70^\circ &= \cos 10^\circ \cdot \frac{\sqrt{3}}{2} \cdot \frac{1}{2} (\cos 120^\circ + \cos 20^\circ) \\
&= \frac{\sqrt{3}}{4} \cos 10^\circ (\cos 120^\circ + \cos 20^\circ) \\
&= \frac{\sqrt{3}}{4} (\cos 10^\circ \cos 120^\circ + \cos 10^\circ \cos 20^\circ) \\
&= \frac{\sqrt{3}}{4} \left( -\frac{1}{2} \cos 10^\circ + \cos 10^\circ \cos 20^\circ \right).
\end{align*}Applying the product-to-sum formula again, we get
\begin{align*}
\frac{\sqrt{3}}{4} \left( -\frac{1}{2} \cos 10^\circ + \cos 10^\circ \cos 20^\circ \right) &= \frac{\sqrt{3}}{4} \left( -\frac{1}{2} \cos 10^\circ + \frac{\cos 30^\circ + \cos 10^\circ}{2} \right) \\
&= \frac{\sqrt{3}}{8} \cos 30^\circ \\
&= \boxed{\frac{3}{16}}.
\end{align*}

We note that $\cos x = \sin(90^\circ-x),$ so $\cos 10^\circ \cos 70^\circ = \sin 20^\circ \sin 80^\circ$ and $\cos 30^\circ \cos 50^\circ = \frac{1}{2} ( \cos 20^\circ + \cos 80^\circ ).$ Therefore, the given expression becomes \begin{align*}
\cos 10^\circ \cos 30^\circ \cos 50^\circ \cos 70^\circ &= (2 \sin 20^\circ \sin 80^\circ)(\frac{1}{2}\cos 20^\circ + \frac{1}{2}\cos 80^\circ) \\
&= \sin 20^\circ \cos 20^\circ \sin 80^\circ +\sin 80^\circ \cos 20^\circ \sin 20^\circ \\
&= \frac{1}{2} \sin 40^\circ \sin 80^\circ \\
&= \boxed{\frac{1}{8}}.
\end{align*}

The set $S$ consists of all points in the first quadrant that lie between the circles $x^2 + y^2 = 1$ and $x^2 + y^2 = 4.$

[asy]
unitsize(1.5 cm);

filldraw(arc((0,0),1,0,90)--arc((0,0),2,90,0)--cycle,gray(0.7));
draw((-0.5,0)--(2.5,0));
draw((0,-0.5)--(0,2.5));

label("$1$", (1,0), S);
label("$2$", (2,0), S);
[/asy]

Hence, the area of $S$ is
\[\frac{4 \pi - \pi}{4} = \boxed{\frac{3 \pi}{4}}.\]

The region $S$ is shown below.

[asy]
unitsize(2 cm);

fill((1,0)--(2,0)--(2,sqrt(2))--(1,sqrt(2)/2)--cycle,gray(0.7));
draw(Circle((0,0),1));
draw(Circle((0,0),2));
draw((1,0)--(1,sqrt(2)/2)--(2,sqrt(2)),red);
draw((2,0)--(2,sqrt(2)));
draw((0,0)--(2,0));
draw((0,0)--(0,2.2));

label("$r = 1$", (1,-0.2), S);
label("$r = 2$", (2,-0.2), S);

label("$\theta = \frac{\pi}{2}$", (0.4,1));
[/asy]

To describe the region $S$ using Cartesian coordinates, we let $x = r \cos \theta$ and $y = r \sin \theta.$  Then $1 \le r \le 2$ and $0 \le \theta \le \frac{\pi}{2}$ become $1 \le x^2 + y^2 \le 4$ and $y \ge 0.$

Hence, the region $S$ is described by $1 \le x^2 + y^2 \le 4$ and $y \ge 0.$  This region is shown below.

[asy]
unitsize(2 cm);

fill((1,0)--(2,0)--(2,sqrt(2))--(1,sqrt(2)/2)--cycle,gray(0.7));
draw(Circle((0,0),1));
draw(Circle((0,0),2));
draw((1,0)--(1,sqrt(2)/2)--(2,sqrt(2)),red);
draw((2,0)--(2,sqrt(2)));
draw((0,0)--(2,0));
draw((0,0)--(0,2.2));

filldraw(arc((0,0),(2,0),(-60:60))--(1,sqrt(3))--cycle,gray(0.7));
draw((0,0)--(1,sqrt(3)));
draw((1,sqrt(3))--(1,0));
draw((-1.2,0)--(2.2,0));
draw((0,-0.2)--(0,2.2));

label("$r = 1$", (1,-0.2), S);
label("$r = 2$", (2,-0.2), S);

label("$\theta = \frac{\pi}{2}$", (0.4,1));
label("$y = \sqrt{3}$", (2.2,sqrt(3)));
[/asy]

Thus, the area of $S$ is equal to the area of the shaded region above.  To find this area, we can find the area of the trapezoid with bases $1$ and $\sqrt{3},$ and height $2,$ then subtract the area of the circular sector.  The area of the trapezoid is
\[\frac{1 + \sqrt{3}}{2} \cdot 2 = 1 + \sqrt{3}.\][asy]
unitsize(1.5 cm);

filldraw(arc((0,0),sqrt(3),(30:60))--(sqrt(3),0)--(1,0)--cycle,gray(0.7));
draw(Circle((0,0),sqrt(3)));
draw((0,0)--(sqrt(3),0)--(1,0)--cycle);
draw((sqrt(3),0)--(sqrt(3)/2,sqrt(3)/2)--(0,0));
draw((0,-0.2)--(0,sqrt(3) + 0.2));
draw((-0.2,0)--(sqrt(3) + 0.2,0));

label("$1$", (1/2,0), S);
label("$\sqrt{3}$", (sqrt(3)/2,0), S);
label("$2$", (sqrt(3)/2,sqrt(3)/2), NW);
label("$\frac{\pi}{6}$", (sqrt(3)/2 - 0.1,-0.1));
[/asy]The area of the sector is $\frac{\pi}{6} \cdot \sqrt{3}^2 = \frac{3 \pi}{2},$ so the area of the shaded region is
\[1 + \sqrt{3} - \frac{3 \pi}{2}.\]Hence, the area of $S$ is $\boxed{1 + \sqrt{3} - \frac{3 \pi}{2}}.$

A $60^\circ$ rotation around the origin in the counter-clockwise direction corresponds to multiplication by $\operatorname{cis} 60^\circ = \frac{1}{2} + \frac{\sqrt{3}}{2} i.$

[asy]
unitsize(0.5 cm);

pair A = (3*sqrt(3),-5), B = rotate(60)*(A);

draw((-2,0)--(8,0));
draw((0,-6)--(0,3));
draw((0,0)--A,dashed);
draw((0,0)--B,dashed);

dot("$3 \sqrt{3} - 5i$", A, S);
dot("$4 \sqrt{3} + 2i$", (4*sqrt(3),2), NE);
[/asy]

Thus, the image of $3 \sqrt{3} - 5i$ is
\[(3 \sqrt{3} - 5i) \left( \frac{1}{2} + \frac{\sqrt{3}}{2} i \right) = \boxed{4 \sqrt{3} + 2i}.\]

Sure! Let's work through this problem together. 

First, we need to understand that a $60^\circ$ counterclockwise rotation around the origin can be represented as multiplying a complex number by $e^{i\theta},$ where $\theta = 60^\circ = \frac{\pi}{3}$ radians. 

So, to find the resulting complex number after the rotation, we can simply multiply $3 \sqrt{3} - 5i$ by $e^{i\frac{\pi}{3}}:$

\begin{align*}
(3 \sqrt{3} - 5i)e^{i\frac{\pi}{3}} &= (3 \sqrt{3} - 5i)\left(\frac{1}{2} + \frac{\sqrt{3}}{2}i\right) \\
&= \frac{3}{2}\sqrt{3} + \frac{5}{2}i.
\end{align*}

Therefore, the resulting complex number after the $60^\circ$ counterclockwise rotation is $\boxed{\frac{3}{2}\sqrt{3} + \frac{5}{2}i}.$

The corresponding vectors are $\begin{pmatrix} 0 \\ -2 \\ -5 \end{pmatrix}$ and $\begin{pmatrix} 3 \\ 0 \\ -1 \end{pmatrix},$ so the line can be parameterized by
\[\begin{pmatrix} 0 \\ -2 \\ -5 \end{pmatrix} + t \left( \begin{pmatrix} 3 \\ 0 \\ -1 \end{pmatrix} - \begin{pmatrix} 0 \\ -2 \\ -5 \end{pmatrix} \right) = \begin{pmatrix} 3t \\ -2 + 2t \\ -5 + 4t \end{pmatrix}.\]When the line intersects the $xy$-plane, the $z$-coordinate is $0.$  Hence, $-5 + 4t = 0,$ so $t = \frac{5}{4}.$  Then the vector becomes

Then the vector corresponding to $\ell$ is parallel to \[\begin{pmatrix} 3 - 0 \\ 0 - (-2) \\ (-1) - (-5) \end{pmatrix} = \begin{pmatrix} 3 \\ 2 \\ 4 \end{pmatrix}.\]Therefore, the line $\ell$ is of the form \[\begin{pmatrix} x \\ y \\ z \end{pmatrix} = \begin{pmatrix} 0 \\ -2 \\ -5 \end{pmatrix} + t \begin{pmatrix} 3 \\ 2 \\ 4 \end{pmatrix}\]for some scalar $t.$  We want to find when $z = 0.$  Thus, we want to solve the equations \[\begin{aligned} -5 + 4t &= 0, \\ t &= \frac{-5}{4}. \end{aligned}\]Then
\[\begin{pmatrix} x \\ y \\ z \end{pmatrix} = \begin{pmatrix} 0 \\ -2 \\ -5 \end{pmatrix} + \frac{-5}{4} \begin{pmatrix} 3 \\ 2 \\ 4 \end{pmatrix} = \boxed{\left(-\frac{15}{4}, -\frac{13}{4}, 0 \right)}.\]

Note the similarity of the recursion to the angle addition identity
\[\tan (x + y) = \frac{\tan x + \tan y}{1 - \tan x \tan y}.\]We can take advantage of this similarity as follows: Let $f_1 = 3,$ $f_2 = 2,$ and let $f_n = f_{n - 1} + f_{n - 2}$ for all $n \ge 3.$  Let $\theta_n = \frac{f_n \pi}{12}.$  Then $\tan \theta_1 = \tan \frac{\pi}{4} = 1$ and $\tan \theta_2 = \tan \frac{\pi}{6} = \frac{1}{\sqrt{3}}.$  Also,
\begin{align*}
\tan \theta_{n + 2} &= \tan (\theta_{n + 1} + \theta_n) \\
&= \frac{\tan \theta_{n + 1} + \tan \theta_n}{1 - \tan \theta_n \tan \theta_{n + 1}}.
\end{align*}Since the sequences $(a_n)$ and $(\tan \theta_n)$ have the same initial terms, and the same recursion, they coincide.

Since $\tan \theta$ is periodic with period $\pi,$ to compute further terms of $\tan \theta_n,$ it suffices to compute $f_n$ modulo 12:
\[
\begin{array}{c|c}
n & f_n \pmod{12} \\ \hline
1 & 3 \\
2 & 2 \\
3 & 5 \\
4 & 7 \\
5 & 0 \\
6 & 7 \\
7 & 7 \\
8 & 2 \\
9 & 9 \\
10 & 11 \\
11 & 8 \\
12 & 7 \\
13 & 3 \\
14 & 10 \\
15 & 1 \\
16 & 11 \\
17 & 0 \\
18 & 11 \\
19 & 11 \\
20 & 10 \\
21 & 9 \\
22 & 7 \\
23 & 4 \\
24 & 11 \\
25 & 3 \\
26 & 2
\end{array}
\]Since $a_{25} \equiv a_1 \pmod{12}$ and $a_{26} \equiv a_2 \pmod{12},$ the sequence modulo 12 become periodic at this point, with period 12.

Therefore,
\[a_{2009} = \tan \theta_{2009} = \tan \theta_5 = \boxed{0}.\]

Sure, I'd be happy to help. Let's start by finding the third term of the sequence, $a_3$: \begin{align*}
a_3 &= \frac{a_1+a_2}{1-a_1a_2} \\
&= \frac{1+\frac{1}{\sqrt{3}}}{1-1\cdot\frac{1}{\sqrt{3}}} \\
&= \frac{1+\frac{1}{\sqrt{3}}}{1-\frac{1}{\sqrt{3}}} \\
&= \frac{(\sqrt{3}+1)(\sqrt{3}-1)}{(\sqrt{3}-1)(\sqrt{3}-1)}} \\
&= \frac{2\sqrt{3}-2}{2} \\
&= \sqrt{3}-1.
\end{align*} Now let's find the fourth term, $a_4$: \begin{align*}
a_4 &= \frac{a_2+a_3}{1-a_2a_3} \\
&= \frac{\frac{1}{\sqrt{3}}+\sqrt{3}-1}{1-\frac{1}{\sqrt{3}}\cdot(\sqrt{3}-1)} \\
&= \frac{\sqrt{3}+\frac{1}{\sqrt{3}}-1}{\frac{2}{\sqrt{3}}} \\
&= \frac{\sqrt{3}}{2}+\frac{1}{2\sqrt{3}}-\frac{1}{\sqrt{3}} \\
&= -\frac{1}{2}+\frac{1}{2\sqrt{3}}.
\end{align*} Notice that $a_5$ is simply $a_3$ again, based on the given recurrence relation. This suggests that the sequence may repeat after this point. Let's try to prove this conjecture by induction. Suppose that $a_{n+2} = a_{n-2}$ and $a_{n+3} = a_{n-1}$ for some $n$. Then $$a_{n+4} = \frac{a_{n+2}+a_{n+3}}{1-a_{n+2}a_{n+3}} = \frac{a_{n-2}+a_{n-1}}{1-a_{n-2}a_{n-1}} = a_{n},$$ and $$a_{n+5} = \frac{a_{n+3}+a_{n+4}}{1-a_{n+3}a_{n+4}} = \frac{a_{n-1}+a_n}{1-a_{n-1}a_n} = a_{n+1}.$$ Thus the conjecture holds for $n+4$ and $n+5$, and therefore for all $n\ge1$ by induction. Since we know that $a_3=\sqrt{3}-1$ and $a_4=-\frac{1}{2}+\frac{1}{2\sqrt{3}}$, we can now determine $a_{2009}$: $$a_{2009} = a_{2005} = a_1 = \boxed{1}.$$

Using the scalar triple product, we have
\[\mathbf{a} \cdot (\mathbf{b} \times \mathbf{c}) = \begin{vmatrix} a_1 & a_2 & a_3 \\ b_1 & b_2 & b_3 \\ c_1 & c_2 & c_3 \end{vmatrix}.\]Hence,
\[\mathbf{a} \cdot \begin{pmatrix} 1 \\ -7 \\ 18 \end{pmatrix} = \begin{vmatrix} a_1 & a_2 & a_3 \\ b_1 & b_2 & b_3 \\ c_1 & c_2 & c_3 \end{vmatrix}.\][From this we can find (a dot b) X c]
Similarly,
\[\mathbf{b} \cdot (\mathbf{c} \times \mathbf{a}) = \begin{vmatrix} a_1 & a_2 & a_3 \\ b_1 & b_2 & b_3 \\ c_1 & c_2 & c_3 \end{vmatrix},\]so the determinant on the right-hand side is
\begin{align*}
\begin{vmatrix} a_1 & a_2 & a_3 \\ b_1 & b_2 & b_3 \\ c_1 & c_2 & c_3 \end{vmatrix} &= \mathbf{a} \cdot (\mathbf{b} \times \mathbf{c}) \\
&= \mathbf{a} \cdot \begin{pmatrix} 1 \\ -7 \\ 18 \end{pmatrix} \\
&= 6a_1 - 7a_2 + 3a_3.
\end{align*}Hence,
\begin{align*}
\mathbf{c} \times (3 \mathbf{a} - 2 \mathbf{b}) &= \mathbf{c} \times 3\mathbf{a} - \mathbf{c} \times 2 \mathbf{b} \\
&= 3 \mathbf{c} \times \mathbf{a} - 2 \mathbf{b} \times \mathbf{c} \\
&= 3 \begin{pmatrix} 4 \\ 7 \\ 2 \end{pmatrix} - 2 \begin{pmatrix} 1 \\ -7 \\ 18 \end{pmatrix} \\
&= \boxed{\begin{pmatrix} 10 \\ 35 \\ -26 \end{pmatrix}}.
\end{align*}
