Sure! To convert a point from cylindrical coordinates $(r, \theta, z)$ to rectangular coordinates $(x, y, z)$ we use the following equations:
\begin{align*}
x &= r \cos \theta \\
y &= r \sin \theta \\
z &= z
\end{align*}
In this case, we have $r = 6 \sqrt{3}$, $\theta = \frac{5 \pi}{3}$, and $z = -2$. Substituting these values into the equations above gives:
\begin{align*}
x &= (6 \sqrt{3})\cos\left(\frac{5 \pi}{3}\right) = -9 \\
y &= (6 \sqrt{3})\sin\left(\frac{5 \pi}{3}\right) = -9 \\
z &= -2
\end{align*}
Therefore, the rectangular coordinates of the point are $\left(-9, -9, -2\right)$.

\begin{align*}

Original File: datasets/MATH/MATH/test/precalculus/805.json
Extracting Answer from: 
In general, By DeMoivre's Theorem,
\begin{align*}
\operatorname{cis} n \theta &= (\operatorname{cis} \theta)^n \\
&= (\cos \theta + i \sin \theta)^n \\
&= \cos^n \theta + \binom{n}{1} i \cos^{n - 1} \theta \sin \theta - \binom{n}{2} \cos^{n - 2} \theta \sin^2 \theta - \binom{n}{3} i \cos^{n - 3} \theta \sin^3 \theta + \dotsb.
\end{align*}Matching real and imaginary parts, we get
\begin{align*}
\cos n \theta &= \cos^n \theta - \binom{n}{2} \cos^{n - 2} \theta \sin^2 \theta + \binom{n}{4} \cos^{n - 4} \theta \sin^4 \theta - \dotsb, \\
\sin n \theta &= \binom{n}{1} \cos^{n - 1} \theta \sin \theta - \binom{n}{3} \cos^{n - 3} \theta \sin^3 \theta + \binom{n}{5} \cos^{n - 5} \theta \sin^5 \theta - \dotsb.
\end{align*}Therefore,
\begin{align*}
\tan n \theta &= \frac{\sin n \theta}{\cos n \theta} \\
&= \frac{\dbinom{n}{1} \cos^{n - 1} \theta \sin \theta - \dbinom{n}{3} \cos^{n - 3} \theta \sin^3 \theta + \dbinom{n}{5} \cos^{n - 5} \theta \sin^5 \theta - \dotsb}{\cos^n \theta - \dbinom{n}{2} \cos^{n - 2} \theta \sin^2 \theta + \dbinom{n}{4} \cos^{n - 4} \theta \sin^4 \theta - \dotsb} \\
&= \frac{\dbinom{n}{1} \tan \theta - \dbinom{n}{3} \tan^3 \theta + \dbinom{n}{5} \tan^5 \theta - \dotsb}{1 - \dbinom{n}{2} \tan^2 \theta + \dbinom{n}{4} \tan^4 \theta - \dotsb}.
\end{align*}Taking $n = 9,$ we get
\[\tan 9 \theta = \frac{9 \tan \theta - 84 \tan^3 \theta + 126 \tan^5 \theta - 36 \tan^7 \theta + \tan^9 \theta}{1 - 36 \tan^2 \theta + 126 \tan^4 \theta - 84 \tan^6 \theta + 9 \tan^8 \theta}.\]Note that for $\theta = 5^\circ,$ $25^\circ,$ $\dots,$ $165^\circ,$ $\tan 9 \theta = \tan 45^\circ = 1.$  Thus,
\[1 = \frac{9 \tan \theta - 84 \tan^3 \theta + 126 \tan^5 \theta - 36 \tan^7 \theta + \tan^9 \theta}{1 - 36 \tan^2 \theta + 126 \tan^4 \theta - 84 \tan^6 \theta + 9 \tan^8 \theta}.\]Let $t = \tan \theta,$ so
\[1 = \frac{9t - 84t^3 + 126t^5 - 36t^7 + t^9}{1 - 36t^2 + 126t^4 - 84t^6 + 9t^8}.\]Thus, $\tan 5^\circ,$ $\tan 25^\circ,$ $\dots,$ $\tan 165^\circ$ are the roots of
\[t^9 - 9t^8 - 36t^7 + 84t^6 + 126t^5 - 126t^4 - 84t^3 + 36t^2 + 9t - 1 = 0.\]By Vieta's formulas, their sum is $\boxed{9}.$

We can write
\begin{align*}
\tan 5^\circ &= \frac{\sin 5^\circ}{\cos 5^\circ}, \\
\tan 25^\circ &= \frac{\sin 25^\circ}{\cos 25^\circ}, \\
\tan 45^\circ &= 1, \\
\tan 65^\circ &= \frac{\sin 65^\circ}{\cos 65^\circ} = \frac{\cos 25^\circ}{\sin 25^\circ}, \\
\tan 85^\circ &= \frac{\sin 85^\circ}{\cos 85^\circ} = \frac{\cos 5^\circ}{\sin 5^\circ}.
\end{align*}Then
\begin{align*}
&\tan 5^\circ + \tan 25^\circ + \tan 45^\circ + \dots + \tan 165^\circ \\
&= \frac{\sin 5^\circ}{\cos 5^\circ} + \frac{\sin 25^\circ}{\cos 25^\circ} + 1 + \frac{\cos 25^\circ}{\sin 25^\circ} + \frac{\cos 5^\circ}{\sin 5^\circ} \\
&= \frac{\sin^2 5^\circ \cos 25^\circ + \sin^2 25^\circ \cos 5^\circ + \sin 5^\circ \sin 25^\circ (\cos 5^\circ \cos 25^\circ + \sin 5^\circ \sin 25^\circ)}{\sin 5^\circ \cos 5^\circ \sin 25^\circ \cos 25^\circ} + 1 \\
&= \frac{\sin^2 5^\circ \cos 25^\circ + \sin^2 25^\circ \cos 5^\circ + \frac{1}{2} \sin 10^\circ}{\frac{1}{2} \sin 10^\circ} + 1 \\
&= \left( 2 \cdot \frac{\sin^2 5^\circ + \sin^2 25^\circ + \frac{1}{4} \sin^2 20^\circ}{\frac{1}{2} \sin 10^\circ} \right) + 1 \\
&= \boxed{33}.
\end{align*}The identity we used is $\cos \alpha \cos \beta = \frac{1}{2} [\cos (\alpha - \beta) + \cos (\alpha + \beta)].$  From this,
\begin{align*}
\sin 5^\circ \sin 25^\circ &= \frac{1}{2} [\cos 20^\circ - \cos 30^\circ] \\
&= \frac{1}{2} [\cos 20^\circ - \frac{\sqrt{3}}{2}].
\end{align*}Squaring both sides of the equation $\cos \alpha \cos \beta = \frac{1}{2} [\cos (\alpha - \beta) + \cos (\alpha + \beta)]$ gives us
\[\cos^2 \alpha \cos^2 \beta = \frac{1}{4} [\cos^2 (\alpha - \beta) + 2 \cos (\alpha - \beta) \cos (\alpha + \beta) + \cos^2 (\alpha + \beta)].\]Setting $\alpha = 20^\circ,$ $\beta = 5^\circ$ and adding the two equations gives us
\begin{align*}
\sin^2 5^\circ \cos^2 25^\circ &= \frac{1}{4} [\cos^2 15^\circ + 2 \cos^2 20^\circ + \cos^2 25^\circ] \\
&= \frac{1}{4} \left[ \frac{1}{4} + 2 \cdot \frac{3}{4} - \frac{\sqrt{6} + \sqrt{2}}{4} \right] \\
&= \frac{5 - \sqrt{6} - \sqrt{2}}{16}.
\end{align*}Also,
\begin{align*}
\sin^2 25^\circ &= \frac{1 - \cos 50^\circ}{2} \\
&= \frac{1}{2} - \frac{\sqrt{3}}{4}.
\end{align*}From $\cos 15^\circ = \frac{\sqrt{6} - \sqrt{2}}{4},$ we get
\begin{align*}
\sin^2 20^\circ &= 1 - \cos^2 20^\circ \\
&= 1 - \frac{\cos 40^\circ + 1}{2} \\
&= \frac{3}{4} - \frac{1}{2} \cos 40^\circ.
\end{align*}But
\[\cos 40^\circ = 2 \cos^2 20^\circ - 1,\]so
\begin{align*}
\sin^2 20^\circ &= \frac{3}{4} - \frac{1}{2} (2 \cos^2 20^\circ - 1) \\
&= \frac{1}{2} - \frac{1}{2} \cos^2 20^\circ,
\end{align*}so $\cos^2 20^\circ = \frac{3}{4} - 2 \sin^2 20^\circ.$  Therefore,
\begin{align*}
\sin^2 5^\circ \cos^2 25^\circ &= \sin^2 5^\circ (1 - \sin^2 25^\circ) \\
&= \sin^2 5^\circ - \sin^2 5^\circ \sin^2 25^\circ \\
&= \sin^2 5^\circ - \sin^2 25^\circ \cos^2 5^\circ \\
&= \sin^2 5^\circ - (\frac{1}{2} - \frac{\sqrt{3}}{4} \sin 10^\circ)^2 \cos^2 5^\circ \\
&= \sin^2 5^\circ - (\frac{1}{4} \cos^2 5^\circ - \frac{\sqrt{3}}{4} \sin 10^\circ \cos^2 5^\circ + \frac{3}{16} \sin^2 10^\circ) \\
&= \left( \frac{1}{2} - \frac{\sqrt{3}}{4} \sin 10^\circ \right) \sin 10^\circ + \frac{1}{8} \cos^2 5^\circ \\
&= \frac{1}{2} \sin 10^\circ + \frac{1}{8} (1 - \sin^2 5^\circ) \\
&= \frac{1}{2} \sin 10^\circ + \frac{1}{8} \cos^2 5^\circ.
\end{align*}By the double angle formula,
\begin{align*}
\cos 10^\circ &= 1 - 2 \sin^2 5^\circ, \\
\cos 20^\circ &= 2 \cos^2 10^\circ - 1.
\end{align*}Then
\begin{align*}
\sin^2 5^\circ + \cos^2 5^\circ &= 1, \\
\cos^2 5^\circ + \cos^2 10^\circ &= \frac{5}{4}, \\
\cos^2 10^\circ + \cos^2 20^\circ &= \frac{9}{4}.
\end{align*}Also, $\sin 10^\circ = 2 \cos 40^\circ \sin 20^\circ,$ so
\[\sin^2 10^\circ = 4 \cos^2 40^\circ \sin^2 20^\circ = 4 \cos^2 20^\circ (1 - \cos^2 20^\circ).\]

Multiplying both sides by $zw(z + w),$ we get
\[zw = (z + w)^2,\]which simplifies to $w^2 + zw + z^2 = 0.$  By the quadratic formula,
\[w = \frac{-1 \pm i \sqrt{3}}{2} \cdot z,\]so the solutions are $w = z \operatorname{cis} 120^\circ$ and $w = z \operatorname{cis} 240^\circ,$ which means that $P$ is an equilateral triangle.

[asy]
usepackage("amsmath");

unitsize(2 cm);

pair A, B, C;

A = dir(20);
B = dir(20 + 120);
C = dir(20 + 240);

draw(Circle((0,0),1));
draw(A--B--C--cycle);
draw((-1.2,0)--(1.2,0));
draw((0,-1.2)--(0,1.2));

dot("$z$", A, A);
dot("$z \operatorname{cis} 120^\circ$", B, B);
dot("$z \operatorname{cis} 240^\circ$", C, SW);
[/asy]

The side length of the equilateral triangle is
\[\left| z - \frac{-1 + i \sqrt{3}}{2} z \right| = \left| \frac{3 - i \sqrt{3}}{2} \right| |z| = \sqrt{3} \cdot 2,\]so the area of the equilateral triangle is
\[\frac{\sqrt{3}}{4} \cdot (2 \sqrt{3})^2 = \boxed{3 \sqrt{3}}.\]

First, we simplify the given equation. Multiplying both sides by $z(w+z)$ gives
\[w+z = w(z+w),\]which simplifies to $w^2+(1-z)w+z^2=0.$
By the quadratic formula,
\[w = \frac{-1+z\pm\sqrt{(1-z)^2-4z^2}}{2} = \frac{-1+z\pm i\sqrt{3}z}{2}.\]Furthermore, since $|w| = \left|\frac{1}{z+w}\right| = \left|\frac{1}{2z}\right| = \frac{1}{2},$ it follows that the three solutions for $w$ are numbers on the circle centered at the origin with radius $\frac{1}{2}.$ We want to find the area enclosed by the triangle $OZW$ (which is similar to an equilateral triangle with side length 1), as in the diagram below:
[asy]
unitsize(1.5 cm);

pair A, O, W, Z;

O = (0,0);
Z = 2*dir(36);
A = (-sqrt(3),1)/2;
W = reflect(O,A)*(Z);

draw(circle(O,2));
draw(A--O--W--Z--O);
draw(O--(O + dir(36)));
draw(O--(A+W)/2);
draw(rightanglemark(O,(A+W)/2,Z,5));

label("$O$", O, SW, fontsize(10));
label("$Z$", Z, N, fontsize(10));
label("$W$", W, NE, fontsize(10));
label("$A$", A, NW, fontsize(10));
[/asy]
Note that $\angle OZW = 60^\circ,$ so the area of the region bounded by $OZW$ is
\[\frac{[OZW]}{[OZ]^2} \cdot \pi OZ^2 = \frac{1}{4}\cdot\pi\cdot2^2 \cdot \frac{\sqrt{3}}{4} = \boxed{\frac{\pi}{4}\sqrt{3}}.\]

In cylindrical coordinates, $z$ simply denotes the $z$-coordinate of a point.  Thus, for a fixed $z$-coordinate $c,$ all the points lie on a plane that is parallel to the $xy$-plane.  The answer is $\boxed{\text{(C)}}.$

Original File: datasets/MATH/MATH/test/precalculus/83.json
Extracting Answer from: 
The normal vector to the plane will be orthogonal to both
\[\begin{pmatrix} a \\ 1 \\ 1 \end{pmatrix} - \begin{pmatrix} 1 \\ b \\ 1 \end{pmatrix} = \begin{pmatrix} a - 1 \\ 1 - b \\ 0 \end{pmatrix}\]and
\[\begin{pmatrix} a \\ 1 \\ 1 \end{pmatrix} - \begin{pmatrix} 1 \\ 1 \\ c \end{pmatrix} = \begin{pmatrix} a - 1 \\ 0 \\ 1 - c \end{pmatrix}.\]Their cross product is
\[\begin{pmatrix} a - 1 \\ 1 - b \\ 0 \end{pmatrix} \times \begin{pmatrix} a - 1 \\ 0 \\ 1 - c \end{pmatrix} = \begin{pmatrix} (b - 1)(c - 1) \\ (a - 1)(c - 1) \\ (a - 1)(b - 1) \end{pmatrix}.\]By scaling, we can take $\begin{pmatrix} 1/(1 - a) \\ 1/(1 - b) \\ 1/(1 - c) \end{pmatrix}$ as the normal vector.  Since the plane passes through $(0,0,0),$ the equation of the plane is
\[\frac{x}{1 - a} + \frac{y}{1 - b} + \frac{z}{1 - c} = 0.\]Since the plane passes through $(a,1,1),$
\[\frac{a}{1 - a} + \frac{1}{1 - b} + \frac{1}{1 - c} = 0.\]Adding 1 to both sides, we get
\[\frac{a}{1 - a} + 1 + \frac{1}{1 - b} + \frac{1}{1 - c} = 1,\]so
\[\frac{1}{1 - a} + \frac{1}{1 - b} + \frac{1}{1 - c} = \boxed{1}.\]

Extracting Answer from: 
Since the points $(0,0,0),$ $(a,1,1),$ $(1,b,1),$ and $(1,1,c)$ are coplanar, the vectors $\begin{pmatrix} a \\ 1 \\ 1 \end{pmatrix},$ $\begin{pmatrix} 1 \\ b \\ 1 \end{pmatrix},$ and $\begin{pmatrix} 1 \\ 1 \\ c \end{pmatrix} - \begin{pmatrix} 1 \\ b \\ 1 \end{pmatrix} = \begin{pmatrix} 0 \\ 1-b \\ c-1 \end{pmatrix}$ are linearly dependent.  In other words, there exist constants $s,$ $t,$ and $u,$ not all zero, such that
\[s \begin{pmatrix} a \\ 1 \\ 1 \end{pmatrix} + t \begin{pmatrix} 1 \\ b \\ 1 \end{pmatrix} + u \begin{pmatrix} 0 \\ 1-b \\ c-1 \end{pmatrix} = \begin{pmatrix} 0 \\ 0 \\ 0 \end{pmatrix}.\]Then $sa + t = s + tb = s + (1 - b)u = 1 + tu = 1 + (c - 1)u = ta = (1 - c)s.$  Therefore, $u = \frac{1 + ta - s}{1 - b}$ and $u = \frac{1 - tb - s}{1 - c}.$  Setting these equal, we get
\[\frac{1 + ta - s}{1 - b} = \frac{1 - tb - s}{1 - c}.\]Then $(1 + ta - s)(1 - c) = (1 - tb - s)(1 - b),$ which simplifies to
\[t = \frac{c - ab}{a + b + c - abc}.\]Then
\begin{align*}
\frac{1}{1 - a} + \frac{1}{1 - b} + \frac{1}{1 - c} &= \frac{1 - b + 1 - a + 1 - c}{(1 - a)(1 - b)(1 - c)} \\
&= \frac{2 - a - b - c}{(1 - a)(1 - b)(1 - c)} \\
&= -\frac{abc}{(1 - a)(1 - b)(1 - c)} \\
&= -\frac{t}{1 - a} \\
&= \boxed{\frac{ab - c}{a + b + c - abc}}.
\end{align*}

\[
\begin{vmatrix} 0 & 1 & 1 \\ x + 2 & 7 & -2 \\ x^3 + 8 & 343 & -8 \end{vmatrix}
= 0.\]The first column becomes all zeros when $x = -2,$ so this is another possible value of $x.$

\begin{vmatrix} 0 & 1 & 1 \\ x + 2 & 7 & -2 \\ x^3 + 8 & 343 & -8 \end{vmatrix} &= -(x + 2) \begin{vmatrix} 1 & 1 \\ 343 & -8 \end{vmatrix} + (x^3 + 8) \begin{vmatrix} 1 & 1 \\ 7 & -2 \end{vmatrix} \\

We have that
\[\cos \theta = \frac{\mathbf{a} \cdot (\mathbf{a} + \mathbf{b} + \mathbf{c})}{\|\mathbf{a}\| \|\mathbf{a} + \mathbf{b} + \mathbf{c}\|}.\]Let $d = \|\mathbf{a}\| = \|\mathbf{b}\| = \|\mathbf{c}\|.$  Since $\mathbf{a},$ $\mathbf{b},$ $\mathbf{c}$ are mutually orthogonal, $\mathbf{a} \cdot \mathbf{b} = \mathbf{a} \cdot \mathbf{c} = \mathbf{b} \cdot \mathbf{c} = 0.$  Hence,
\[\mathbf{a} \cdot (\mathbf{a} + \mathbf{b} + \mathbf{c}) = \mathbf{a} \cdot \mathbf{a} + \mathbf{a} \cdot \mathbf{b} + \mathbf{a} \cdot \mathbf{c} = d^2.\]Also,
\begin{align*}
\|\mathbf{a} + \mathbf{b} + \mathbf{c}\|^2 &= (\mathbf{a} + \mathbf{b} + \mathbf{c}) \cdot (\mathbf{a} + \mathbf{b} + \mathbf{c}) \\
&= \mathbf{a} \cdot \mathbf{a} + \mathbf{b} \cdot \mathbf{b} + \mathbf{c} \cdot \mathbf{c} + 2(\mathbf{a} \cdot \mathbf{b} + \mathbf{a} \cdot \mathbf{c} + \mathbf{b} \cdot \mathbf{c}) \\
&= d^2 + d^2 + d^2 \\
&= 3d^2.
\end{align*}Hence, $\|\mathbf{a} + \mathbf{b} + \mathbf{c}\| = d \sqrt{3},$ so
\[\cos \theta = \frac{d^2}{d \cdot d \sqrt{3}} = \boxed{\frac{1}{\sqrt{3}}}.\]

Extracting Answer from: 
Certainly! Let's call the magnitude of the vectors (which are all the same by the problem statement) $\|\mathbf{a}\| = \|\mathbf{b}\| = \|\mathbf{c}\| = x$.

Since any pair of the three vectors are orthogonal, we know that \[\mathbf{a}\cdot\mathbf{b}=\mathbf{a}\cdot\mathbf{c}=\mathbf{b}\cdot\mathbf{c}=0.\]

Now, we want to find the angle between $\mathbf{a}$ and $\mathbf{a}+\mathbf{b}+\mathbf{c}$. We'll call these vectors $\mathbf{v}$ and $\mathbf{w}$, respectively. To do this, we can use the formula \[\cos\theta = \frac{\mathbf{v}\cdot\mathbf{w}}{\|\mathbf{v}\|\|\mathbf{w}\|}.\]

First, we'll find $\mathbf{v}$. \[\mathbf{v} = \mathbf{a}+(\mathbf{b}+\mathbf{c}) = \mathbf{a}+\mathbf{b}+\mathbf{c}.\]Now let's calculate the dot product $\mathbf{a}\cdot(\mathbf{b}+\mathbf{c})$. Note that this is equal to both $\mathbf{a}\cdot\mathbf{b}$ and $\mathbf{a}\cdot\mathbf{c}$ since $\mathbf{b}$ and $\mathbf{c}$ are orthogonal to $\mathbf{a}$, so $\mathbf{a}\cdot(\mathbf{b}+\mathbf{c}) = 0$. Therefore, we have \begin{align*}
\mathbf{v}\cdot\mathbf{v} &= (\mathbf{a}+\mathbf{b}+\mathbf{c})\cdot(\mathbf{a}+\mathbf{b}+\mathbf{c}) \\
&= \mathbf{a}\cdot\mathbf{a} + 2\mathbf{a}\cdot(\mathbf{b}+\mathbf{c}) + (\mathbf{b}+\mathbf{c})\cdot(\mathbf{b}+\mathbf{c}) \\
&= \|\mathbf{a}\|^2 + \|\mathbf{b}+\mathbf{c}\|^2 \\
&= x^2 + (2x)^2 \\
&= 5x^2.
\end{align*}Therefore, $\|\mathbf{v}\| = \sqrt{5}x$.

Finally, we calculate $\mathbf{v}\cdot\mathbf{w}$. Using the distributive property of the dot product, we have \begin{align*}
\mathbf{v}\cdot\mathbf{w} &= (\mathbf{a}+\mathbf{b}+\mathbf{c})\cdot\mathbf{a}+(\mathbf{a}+\mathbf{b}+\mathbf{c})\cdot(\mathbf{b}+\mathbf{c}) \\
&= \mathbf{a}\cdot\mathbf{a} + \mathbf{a}\cdot(\mathbf{b}+\mathbf{c}) \\
&\hspace{10mm}+ (\mathbf{b}+\mathbf{c})\cdot\mathbf{a} + (\mathbf{b}+\mathbf{c})\cdot(\mathbf{b}+\mathbf{c}) \\
&= \|\mathbf{a}\|^2 + \mathbf{a}\cdot(\mathbf{b}+\mathbf{c}) + (\mathbf{b}+\mathbf{c})\cdot\mathbf{a} + \|\mathbf{b}+\mathbf{c}\|^2 \\
&= 2x^2 + (\mathbf{b}+\mathbf{c})\cdot\mathbf{a} + (2x)^2 \\
&= 2x^2 + 0 + 4x^2 \\
&= 6x^2.
\end{align*}Therefore, \[\cos\theta = \frac{\mathbf{v}\cdot\mathbf{w}}{\|\mathbf{v}\|\|\mathbf{w}\|} = \frac{6x^2}{(\sqrt{5}x)(\|\mathbf{a}+\mathbf{b}+\mathbf{c}\|)}.\]But $\|\mathbf{a}+\mathbf{b}+\mathbf{c}\| = \sqrt{(1^2+1^2+1^2)x^2} = \sqrt{3}x$, so we have \[\cos\theta = \boxed{\frac{2\sqrt{3}}{3}}.\]

Extracting Answer from: 
Since $\angle A,$ $\angle B,$ $\angle C$ form an arithmetic sequence, $2 \angle B = \angle A + \angle C.$  Then
\[3 \angle B = \angle A + \angle B + \angle C = 180^\circ,\]which means $\angle B = 60^\circ,$ and $\angle A + \angle C = 120^\circ.$

Let $h$ be the altitude from $B.$

[asy]
unitsize (1 cm);

pair A, B, C, D;

A = (0,0);
B = 5*dir(40);
C = (5,0);
D = (B.x,0);

draw(A--B--C--cycle);
draw(B--D);

label("$A$", A, SW);
label("$B$", B, N);
label("$C$", C, SE);
label("$h$", (B + D)/2, E);
[/asy]

Then
\[h = AB - BC = \frac{h}{\sin A} - \frac{h}{\sin C},\]so $1 = \frac{1}{\sin A} - \frac{1}{\sin C}.$  Hence,
\[\sin C - \sin A = \sin A \sin C.\]We can write this as
\[2 \sin \frac{C - A}{2} \cos \frac{A + C}{2} = \frac{\cos (A - C) - \cos (A + C)}{2}.\]Since $A + C = 120^\circ,$
\[\sin \frac{C - A}{2} = \frac{\cos (A - C) + \frac{1}{2}}{2} = \frac{\cos (C - A) + \frac{1}{2}}{2}.\]Then
\begin{align*}
4 \sin \frac{C - A}{2} &= 2 \cos (C - A) + 1 \\
&= 2 \left( 1 - 2 \sin^2 \frac{C - A}{2} \right) + 1 \\
&= 3 - 4 \sin^2 \frac{C - A}{2},
\end{align*}so
\[4 \sin^2 \frac{C - A}{2} + 4 \sin \frac{C - A}{2} - 3 = 0.\]This factors as
\[\left( 2 \sin \frac{C - A}{2} - 1 \right) \left( 2 \sin \frac{C - A}{2} + 3 \right) = 0.\]Thus, the only possible value of $\sin \frac{C - A}{2}$ is $\boxed{\frac{1}{2}}.$

Extracting Answer from: 
Let $x$ be the common difference of the angles.  Then $\angle B = \angle A + x$ and $\angle C = \angle A + 2x.$

Let $AB = c,$ $BC = a,$ and $AC = b,$ so by the Law of Cosines,
\begin{align*}
b^2 &= a^2 + c^2 - 2ac \cos B \\
&= a^2 + c^2 - 2ac \cos (A + x) \\
&= a^2 + c^2 - 2ac (\cos A \cos x - \sin A \sin x).
\end{align*}Also, the area of triangle $ABC$ is
\[\frac{1}{2} ab \sin A = \frac{1}{2} ac \sin B.\]Hence,
\[c \sin B = b \sin A.\]Then
\begin{align*}
\sin A &= \sin B \cdot \frac{c}{b} \\
&= \frac{a \sin (A + x)}{b} \\
&= \frac{a (\sin A \cos x + \cos A \sin x)}{b} \\
&= \frac{a \cos x}{b} \sin A + \frac{a \sin x}{b} \cos A.
\end{align*}Hence, $\tan A = \frac{a \sin x}{b - a \cos x}.$  Similarly, from $B = A + x$ and $C = A + 2x,$
\begin{align*}
\tan B &= \frac{b \sin x}{a - b \cos x}, \\
\tan C &= \frac{b \sin (x + A)}{c} \\
&= \frac{b (\sin A \cos x + \cos A \sin x)}{c},
\end{align*}so $\tan C = \frac{a \cos x}{b \sin A} \tan A + \frac{a \sin x}{b}.$

The length of the altitude from $B$ to $\overline{AC}$ is
\[a \sin B = b \sin A.\]Similarly, $AB - BC = c - a,$ so
\[\frac{a \cos x}{b - a \cos x} = c - a.\]Solving for $c,$ we find
\[c = \frac{a + a \cos x}{1 - \cos x}.\]Hence,
\[\frac{1}{2} ac \sin B = a \sin A (\frac{a + a \cos x}{1 - \cos x}) \cdot \frac{b \sin A}{a} = \frac{a^2 b \sin^2 A (1 + \cos x)}{(1 - \cos x)^2}.\]Also,
\[ab \sin C = ab (\sin A \cos x + \cos A \sin x),\]so
\[\frac{ab \sin C}{a + b + c} = \frac{ab (\sin A \cos x + \cos A \sin x)}{a + b + \frac{a + a \cos x}{1 - \cos x}} = \frac{a b \sin A (1 - \cos x)}{(1 + \cos x)^2}.\]Then
\[\frac{1}{2} ac \sin B = \frac{ab \sin C}{a + b + c}\]becomes
\[\frac{a \sin^2 A (1 + \cos x)}{(1 - \cos x)^2} = \frac{a \sin A (1 - \cos x)}{(1 + \cos x)^2}.\]Since $\sin A \neq 0,$
\[\frac{(1 + \cos x)}{(1 - \cos x)^2} = \frac{1}{(1 + \cos x)^2}.\]This simplifies to $(\cos x - 1)(5 \cos x + 3) = 0.$  Hence, $\cos x = 1$ or $\cos x = -\frac{3}{5}.$

If $\cos x = 1,$ then $\triangle ABC$ is isosceles, which forces $\angle A = \angle B = \angle C = 60^\circ.$  Then $\sin \frac{C - A}{2} = \sin 0^\circ = 0.$

If $\cos x = -\frac{3}{5},$ then from $\frac{a \cos x}{b - a \cos x} = c - a,$ $c = \frac{5a}{8}.$  Then by the Law of Cosines on triangle $ABC,$
\[b^2 = a^2 + c^2 - 2ac \cos B = a^2 + \frac{25}{64} a^2 + \frac{15}{16} a^2 = 2a^2.\]Hence, $b = a \sqrt{2},$ so $\sin A = \frac{a \sqrt{2}}{2a} = \frac{1}{\sqrt{2}}.$  Then
\[\sin \frac{C - A}{2} = \sin \frac{3A - C}{4} = \pm \cos \frac{\pi}{4} = \pm \frac{1}{\sqrt{2}}.\]Therefore, the possible values of $\sin \frac{C - A}{2}$ are $\boxed{0, \pm \frac{1}{\sqrt{2}}}.$

Extracting Answer from: 
Certainly! We can solve this problem using the Cauchy-Schwarz inequality:

\[\mathbf{v} \cdot \mathbf{w} \leq \|\mathbf{v}\| \|\mathbf{w}\|.\]

In our case, this implies

\[\mathbf{v} \cdot \mathbf{w} \leq 3 \cdot 4 = 12.\]

Furthermore, the inequality is an equality if and only if $\mathbf{v}$ and $\mathbf{w}$ are linearly dependent, which in turn means that one of the vectors is a scalar multiple of the other. To see why this is true, consider the vectors $\mathbf{u} = t\mathbf{v} - \mathbf{w}$, where $t$ is a scalar. Then we have

\begin{align*}
\|\mathbf{u}\|^2 &= (\mathbf{u} \cdot \mathbf{u}) \\
&= (t\mathbf{v} - \mathbf{w}) \cdot (t\mathbf{v} - \mathbf{w}) \\
&= t^2 \|\mathbf{v}\|^2 - 2t(\mathbf{v} \cdot \mathbf{w}) + \|\mathbf{w}\|^2.
\end{align*}

We want to minimize $\mathbf{v} \cdot \mathbf{w}$ subject to the constraint that $\|\mathbf{v}\| = 3$. Using the above argument, we can see that this is equivalent to minimizing $\|\mathbf{u}\|^2$ subject to the constraint $t^2 \|\mathbf{v}\|^2 = 9$. But by the Cauchy-Schwarz inequality, we have $\|\mathbf{u}\|^2 \geq 0$ for all vectors $\mathbf{u}$, so the minimum value of $\|\mathbf{u}\|^2$ is $0$ and is achieved if and only if $\mathbf{u} = \mathbf{0}$ or $\mathbf{v}$ and $\mathbf{w}$ are linearly dependent. In other words, the minimum value of $\mathbf{v} \cdot \mathbf{w}$ subject to the constraint $\|\mathbf{v}\| = 3$ is $\boxed{12}$, which is attained if and only if $\mathbf{v}$ and $\mathbf{w}$ are collinear.

\[\renewcommand{\arraystretch}{1.5} \begin{pmatrix} \frac{13}{14} & -\frac{1}{7} & \frac{3}{14} \\ -\frac{1}{7} & \frac{5}{7} & \frac{3}{7} \\ \frac{3}{14} & \frac{3}{7} & \frac{5}{14} \end{pmatrix} \renewcommand{\arraystretch}{1} \begin{pmatrix} a \\ b \\ c \end{pmatrix} = \begin{pmatrix} 0 \\ 0 \\ 0 \end{pmatrix}.\]Then $\frac{13}{14} a - \frac{1}{7} b + \frac{3}{14} = 0,$ $-\frac{1}{7} a + \frac{5}{7} b + \frac{3}{7} c = 0,$ and $\frac{3}{14} a + \frac{3}{7} b + \frac{5}{14} = 0.$  These reduce to
\begin{align*}

-a + 5b + 3c &= 0, \\
3a + 6b + 5c &= 0.
\end{align*}Subtracting the first two equations, we get $14a - 7b = 0,$ so $b = 2a.$  Then
\[-a + 10a + 3c = 0,\]so $c = -3a.$  Hence,
\[\begin{pmatrix} a \\ b \\ c \end{pmatrix} = \begin{pmatrix} a \\ 2a \\ -3a \end{pmatrix} = a \begin{pmatrix} 1 \\ 2 \\ -3 \end{pmatrix},\]so the vector we seek is $\boxed{\begin{pmatrix} 1 \\ 2 \\ -3 \end{pmatrix}}.$
